A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash value of the Bonus for 1*33.', the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these policies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments—more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

A GRIM REVENGE.
Wife : “ Now, this is the third time I’ve caught you in the kitchen talking to
■' the cook.”
' Husband : “Yes, I—l believe it is.”
Wife ; “ Well, the next time I catch you talking to the cook I’ll discharge her and
do the cooking myself.”—(That cured him.)

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in tae
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Ncivs-paper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin'” will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney, In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, APRIL 16, 1892.
“The Bulletin” Votes for Shiels.
In a few days the makeshift Ministry now
reigning in Victoria will go before the
country, and whether it will come back with
a strong majority or fail to come back at all
is at present a matter of some conjecture.
There are no reliable signs either way ; to
all appearance the entire province is sitting
on a rail and waiting with an almost entire
absence of enthusiasm for the result, pre-
pared to accept it with equanimity, whatever
it may be. The Shiels Cabinet came into
power almost entirely by accident, and it
has had little chance, so far, to show its real
capacity. The two Cabinets which preceded
it were also, more or less, accidental. Gillies
obtained the Premiership because of Ser-
vice’s retirement and Berry’s appointment
of himself to be Agent-General in London.
The country showed no definite signs of
wanting Gillies, and never appointed him
to his high office—it merely accepted him as
a painful fact, and the situation grew upon
him, in a manner of speaking, like moss or
any other excrescence. Munro obtained the
leadership of the Opposition because the
party had never seen him in office, but im-
agined from his wooden aspect and hardness
of expression that he couldn’t be worse than
Bent, whom it had seen; and he inherited the
shoes of the man of “ recoups.” The country
never expressed its confidence in Munro at
a general election, and he fled from the
scene before it had a chance to do so, there-
fore he also might be fairly classed as an
accident. When he formed his Cabinet he
adhered strictly to untried men. Ihe few
old office-holders surviving among his party
had mostly proved themselves so exceedingly
foolish that he preferred to distribute the
portfolios among men who had had no chance
to exhibit whatever foolishness they might
possess. The abject dullness of Victorian
politics in the days of the Coalition, when
the whole science of Government was to
hang on and promise new railways, gave
no scope for genius, and in that utterly
dreary Legislature Demosthenes and John
Nimmo would have stood very much on a
level—the latter because he couldn’t speak,
and the former because he had practically
nothing to speak about. Therefore, Munro,
having few' brilliant men to choose from, and
being utterly incapable of recognising
brilliance in any case, followed the safe rule
of choosing such colleagues as had not yet
been found out too conspicuously, and when
he escaped to England amid a chaos of
bursting banks and financial disasters his
colleagues scrambled for his billet, and
Shiels got it. The country never definitely
demanded Shiels any more than it
did Munuo or Gillies; in fact, the
province, for nine years past, has never
expressed itself very definitely about any-
thing.
* * #
Therefore, when Graham Berry re-
appeared on the scene, he came with an
obvious resolution to brush away this new
politician, and assume, by a species of divine
right, Ins. old position at the head of the
State. Excepting Shiels, there was no one
in the way. Service was old, worn-out, a
Tory, and not desirous of office. Gillies,
who was desirous of office—who, in fact,
would gladly absorb forty offices—had fallen
with a crash into obscurity. Deakin, from
whom the democracy once expected much,
had been for eight years eternally on the
verge of doing something, and yet never had
done anything; either the incubus of Gillies
had utterly weighed him down, or else the
country had been deceived by his wise
aspect and his spectacles from the very
beginning. Patterson, from whom the
democracy never expected anything, had
developed a certain utterly brutal stupidity
which made him objectionable to all parties.
The nation had long since ceased to take
0 Loohlen seriously, and only tolerated him
as a harmless and gentlemanly object of
derision. And the daily papers, because
they were so long accustomed to the worn-
out, toothless, political hacks who had buzzed
aimlessly round the Treasury benches for
many years, had at last got into the habit of
thinking that fat and dull respectability
and extreme old age were the only
possible signs of political wisdom ; there-
fore, they habitually alluded to the Shiels
Cabinet as a species of joke, by reason of its
youthfulness and inexperience; and because,
in the course of its few weeks’ reign, it had
never met with any chance of displaying its
capacity, they took it for granted that it had
no capacity to display. The idea that a man
who had not pottered drearily about the
House for twenty years, and grown old and
muddled in the refreshment-room, could be
a capable head of the State, apparently
never occurred to anybody. Shiels had
risen suddenly and by chance, and the
country being unaccustomed to anything
but plodding mediocrity, it was cheerfully
assumed that such a chance never came to
anyone but a fool. Berry, apparently,
assumed this, and the ancient renegade from
the people’s cause, with his shoddy title and
his obtrusive “ loyalty,” came back with an
undisguised conviction that he was the man
of the hour, and that the country would
elect him as a shrieking reactionary merely
because it had elected him before as an
uncompromising democrat, and been sold.
And so profound was the public faith in
septuagenarian toothlessness and rheumatism
as the concomitants of statesmanship that
it was half believed in many quarters that
the Shiels Cabinet would collapse at the
sight of Berry, without a struggle ; and the
Premier’s contemptuous remark that the
country was not necessarily dependent on
aged persons with artificial molars, came
upon a large number of his hearers as a
surprise and an awakening.
* # #
Practically—though not ostensibly—the
contest lies between Shiels and Berry, and
that the propertied classes will give their
solid support to the latter is almost a
matter of certainty. Whether the Victorian
democratic party will give an equally solid
support to the Premier is at present a
matter of doubt, but if there is any sense or
foresight or political capacity among the
masses they will do so. Shiels is not an
ideal democrat, but he is the nearest approach
to one that can be obtained, and the Labour
Party, the Republicans, Land Nationalism's,
and other progressive elements of the com-
munity must either vote as one man for the
best democrat who is to be had, or else, by
their dissensions, allow the power to drift
into the hands of the “National” Association
and its prophets. And it must be remem-
bered in his favour that Premier Shiels has
done more in his brief career for the demo-
cratic cause than 90 per cent, of the profes-
sional democrats of Australia. The Divorce
Bill, which stands to his credit in the Vic-
torian statute-book, is in itself a greater
benefit to the public than all that was accom-
plished by Graham Berry in all his long
and stormy existence as a Tribune of
the People. The Victorians have still a
vague and hazy reverence for the latter
individual because he promised to “ burst up
the big estates,” which estates remain un-
burst to this day - because he. or one of his
Ministers, threatened to ship the Governor
back to his native land, and did not ship
him—because he announced a programme of
broken heads and flaming houses, and yet no
heads were broken, and no houses flamed in
any part of the province. And it has a
vague and hazy contempt for Shiels because
he never stumped the country in a frenzy
with his hair standing on end and a
trail of broken promises and extinguished
fireworks in his wake. But it is for
the nation to remember that even when
Berry was a democrat upon the stump
he did practically nothing for anybody save
himself, and now that he is a lurid Tory and
too old to go upon the stump any longer
there is absolutely nothing to be hoped for
from him or any party with which he is
associated. The choice lies between the man
who promised much and did nothing, and the
man who, so far, has promised little and kept
his pledges to the uttermost.
# # »
The programme of the Shield Govern-
ment, though far from complete in many
respects, is much the best that has yet
appeared in Victoria. It includes the
abolition of the bogus banks which prey
upon the working classes ; an income-
tax, and a tax on the unimproved
value of land—both of which tend to shift
the State burdens from the poor on to the
rich, while the latter in particular is a step
in the right direction of socialism, and ought
to command the generous support of the
democracy. Also, he propounds a distinct
scheme for the breaking up of the great land
monopoly by the repurchase of estates —to
be paid for by Government inscribed stock—
and their sale (for which leasing should be
substituted) to agriculturists in small allot-
ments on easy terms of payment ; and he
announces himself definitely as a supporter
of the One-man-one-vote policy. More-
over, he is one of the very few Aus-
tralian politicians who pledge themselves
to support this principle as the basis
of the Australian Federation. Against
this there appears the rival policy of
Berry, and of Gillies, that fuzzy old man
whose place Berry will presently Jump,
which consists chiefly in a bald and vague,
not to say melancholy, statement, to the
effect that they believe in making a “ great
effort ”to restore the national credit. The
scheme of the Shiels Government may be
comparatively meagre, but, at all events, it
includes not one proposal of a retrogressive
character. So far as it goes, it is purely
democratic, and the very fact that it contains
no widely reckless promises, is, in itself, a
proof that the Premier has carefully ab-
stained from any attempt to purchase votes
with impossible assurances. And, on the
other side, there is absolutely nothing save
bland and gentle drivel, and old, disused
platitudes of the mildest type.
* # *
There is even yet a possibility that Graham
Berry will attempt to storm the country
with an imposing programme, such as he was
wont to get up in the days of old. If he
does, it can only be hoped that the Victorian
democracy will remember how it trusted to
his garish programmes in the past, and will
refuse to be again defrauded. Whether
Berry ever was anything but a blatant Toiy
at heart, no one can tell; the one absolute
certainty is that he will never be anything
but a Tory in the future. Six long years of
grovel in British conservative circles have
utterly destroyed any popular sympathies he
ever possessed; the K. C.M.G. cannot be born
again in his old age, neither can the ancient
bigot change his spots. The one present
hope of the progressive party in Victoria lies
in the Shiels Government; it may possibly
prove a failure, but the venerable political
hacks who form the only immediate alterna-
tive have all proved themselves failures
already and are foredoomed, every one. The
fact that the present Premier is practically
the creation of an accident does not count in
his disfavour ; the old placemen whom these
provinces have deliberately selected of late
years— Service, Berry, Griffith, M‘ll-
wraith, Parkes, Playford, Vogel, and
others—have done so little good and so much
harm that a politician who came to the
surface in a period of temporary confusion
cannot possibly be worse than the average.
The much advertised circumstance that the
Victorian Ministry consists entirely of new
men, is in itself a reason why it should
receive the ungrudging support of the
democracy ; the old men of Victorian politics
are incurably wedded to the old grooves, and
only a fool hopes to keep a fossil moving in
the paths of progress. Moreover, Premier
Shiels, though not an ideal democi’at, is far
in advance of Victorian democrats in general.
Too much Coalition has reduced the province
to a stolid Conservative state of mind from
which it is only beginning to recover. It
suffers from a surfeit of Union Jackism and
“God Save the Queen/' and has a grovel-
ling tendency to collect subscriptions for
presents to royalty. When it awakes from
this condition it may offer a field for a states-
man of more advanced ideas, but at present
Shiels, who, with a fair trial at the hands
of the people, may expand as he goes along,
is as pronounced a democrat as the country
can well hold.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Kanaka “ Scandals.”
The following parsonical item from Queensland
exhibits the humble expounder in his usual
aspect as a man who always desires to give a
lift to the ricli members of his congregation
At a meeting of the Brisbane Ministers’ Union to-day a
resolution was passed partly affirming the desirableness
of re-introducing black labour, and expn ssing a wish that
all scandals in connection with black labour in Queensland
be done away with.
In other words, the Ministers’ Union wants to
have the leper whitewashed for the future, and
to varnish the loathsome thing so that it will
look outwardly respectable. The real scandal
lies in the fact that the Kanaka doesn’t want
to come, and has to be brought along either by
fraud or violence ; that he doesn’t want to stay
when he has come, and can only be forced to
remain by fine or imprisonment ; that he can’t be
made to sign his three years’ agreement except
by lying and misrepresentation or brutal ter-
rorism ; and that he .dies off at a rate which
makes his employment in the sugar-industry
deliberate murder pure and simple. The Union
is apparently agreeable to all these tlihios
because it can’t have black labour without
them, ami it has got to boom black labour else
the capitalist will quit the church and stop his
subscription. But it wants the “scandals”
connected with the traffic “ done away with ”
which is equivalent to saying that it wants no
row made about them in the papers. Hitherto
whenever a labour ship came back, the captain
and crew have almost invariably gone on
fearsome drunk, and smashed things in
public-houses, and told in hiccoughs grue-
some talcs about their doings among the islands
and the papers have got hold of these facts and
made unpleasant disclosures. Also intrusive
people have gone about at times among the
natives, and interrogated them in their 5 own
language, and collected disagreeable informa-
tion ; and now and then some coroner or ocher
meddlesome public official has inquired about
deaths on the plantations which seemed a little
more ugly and suspicious even than usual. If
the captains and crews can be kept strictly tee-
total and prevented from smashing things and
telling tales, and no one save the planter and
his friends get access to the native employes,
and the coroner has strict instructions to lie
low, and the Registrar-General will keep quiet
and cook the death-records, then there will be
no more Kanaka “scandals.” There never is
any scandal except when somebody digs under the
hearthstone and things are found out. So long
as the kidnapped Polynesian is alluded to only
as a “ benighted brother who has been happily
brought within the pale of Christianity,” and
the nigger who has been killed off on a'planta-
tion is spoken of as a humble friend who has
been removed by Providence from this vale of
tears, and nobody is allowed to use coarse ex-
pressions about cither of them, all will be well.
It is a process of putting roses on the gallows
and painting the lily and the hangman, and
throwing eau-de-cologne over the dead Kanaka,
and making things comfortable for all parties!
Now that the Church is on the job, the deceased
islander will probably get a better tombstone
than he used to do, and an aloe will be planted
over his grave, and an air of piety and collection-
plate will be shed over the scene—but the island
traffic will bo the same old infamy as before,
for it is absolutely incurable. The leper may
be whitewashed, but he remains a leper all the
same.
♦
N S.W. Primrose League.
The New South Wales National Association
wisely selected the sequestered hamlet of Manly,
where the people are “poor and proud,” for
debuting in all the gdazed glory of its political
dickejn In this discretionary valour, it care-
fully imitated its brother-dude of Victoria,
whose eyeglass has always been prominent in
nests of political pimpdom far removed from
the haunts of brawny humanity. As it costs
each visitor a minimum of one shilling to visit
“ Our Village,” the initial meeting of the New
South Wales National Ass. was insured against
disturbance at the hands of the manhood-
suffragists; therefore, the National Ass. rolled
up with confident enthusiasm and invited its
feminine friends to attend its formal christening
and admire its cheap perfumery. There were
quite 60 people present at the meeting, and
more than half of them were ladies. The new r
power is thus seen to be putting forth a giant
strength. It is evidently destined to be the
Primrose League of Australia, so far as any
such Tory institution can flourish in a demo-
cratic community. Its chief function will be to
amuse the less serious hours of an overworked
people, and act as a Punch-and-Judy “com-
bine.” The oratorical heralds of the new move-
ment—if they may be taken seriously—gave
very hazy inklings of their intended programme.
The one-man-one-vote was certainly mentioned
incidentally in a feebly condemnatory fashion,
but even this vague reference drew an interjec-
tion of dissent from one of the score-and-odd
males present. But there w T as a cautious
absence of declaration that the secret fixed-idea
of the National Ass. is to prevent the adoption
of manhood suffrage, and that its one defined
ambition is to confer upon the man who owns
bricks-and-mortar just twice as many votes as the
man who possesses no bricks-and-mortar. Even
wdth its flaccid programme, the meeting w'as a
neat compliment to one of the most venal elector-
ates of New'South Wales—a constituency whose
whole political creed may be summed up in the
desire to get railways and bridges and other
etceteras to improve the values of its private
properties at the expense of the State. Sir
Henry Paukes has recognised this special attri-
bute of Manly on occasions, when he has chosen
it as a safe theatre from which to make public
announcements in the way of experimenting
how the public cat would be likely to jump.
The Manly meeting of the National Ass. was
congenially engineered. The chair was occu-
pied by the local Mayor, a nice-looking poten-
tate, who acts as engineer to the Colonial Sugar
Co. Mr. W. T. Poole, a distinguished “ law-and-
order ” man, moved the first flabby resolution
generalising the “public-confidence’’and “good-
feeling ” cant of the day. Mr, „ Cope, the
seconder of that resolution, is a squatter, an
attorney, an ex-Soudan volunteer, and amateur
officer of strike-time mounted police. Mr.
Adrian Knox, who supported the resolution,
belongs to the same set; Mr. Littlejohn,
another speaker, is a merchant; Mr. A. ConsetT
Stephen, who dittoed his predecessors, is an
attorney ; Mr. W. M. Arnold, another platform
meteor, has succeeded, at the age of 36 years,
in becoming a pensioner on the State ; and the
local secretary of the movement is a bank-clerk
—altogether, a happy Primrose family. We
want to see the “ National ” Ass. hold an open
(not a packed) meeting in Sydney Centennial
Hall.
The Moonta miners, enraged at the failure
of their recent strike, have “ kicked” their Union.
Bad to worse.
Counsel who appeared for a Chow, charged
with perjury at Melbourne Criminal Court, argued
that the prisoner’s limited knowledge of English
made him very liable to say exactly the opposite
of what he really meant. “0, yes, welly good—
ollys mean zackly difHent,” chipped in the China-
man, who had just pleaded “Not guilty,” by the
way.

il ‘ ll j yen in that case he might have refused the

4 lawyer’s bill-of-costs is an artistic branch
feme of the inexact sciences. It is wondrous in
its mazy beauty. Firstly, it is generally for a
'‘lump sum.” But a pitying law permits a client
to demand detailed items. Only, the client so
demanding must pay Bd. a sheet (foolscap) for sa’d
trailing list of details. Whereby his costs are
visibly increased. Next, in case of items being
disputed, the matter goes before the prothonotary
for “taxation.”
If the amount of a bill-of-costs be reduced by tho
taxing-officer by one-sixth of itself, the solicitor
presenting such a bill must pay the costs of the
anneal to the taxing-officer. If the total amount
be not reduced by at least one-sixth, then the
ambling client, in addition to his taxed costs,
has to pay the cost of having had those costs
I 'aSolicitor very seldom indeed has to pay the
costs of any appeal to the taxing-officer. Just
read this i
Lawyer Snipe makes up a bill-of-costs which
comes to (say) £9OO. Nine-tenths of tire items are
downright exactionary (though legal, lawful)
swindles. But as Mr. Taxing-Officer must some-
times do something to justify the payment of his
big salary, Snipe adds (say) .£lOO to the bill in
the form of items which he scarcely expects to
pass the taxing process, yet which, on the other
hand, may possibly squeeze through. Mr. Taxing-
Officer runs down the items, now amounting to a
total of £lOOO, and, feeling unusually merciful
that day, scores out and reduces items to the
amount of (say) £IGO. Now, .as £l6O is not quite
one-sixth of £lOOO, being just £6l3s. 4d. less than
that fractional part, the client has to pay the
costs of taxation. The more audacious and
numerous the absolutely illegal items in a lawyer’s
bill-of-costs are, the more probably will the re-
ductions by the taxing-officer fail to reach the
costs saving proportion, and the more sui'ely will
the client have to pay the costs of his “ con-
tumacy.”
Thus the Law majestically says: “You have
accused an officer of this honourable court of trying
to swindle you, and the taxing-officer has certainly
found him guilty of trying to swindle you. It is
a pretty audacious attempt at a pretty bad
swindle, but not so bad as it might have been,
and it is within the margin of attempted swindle
that is allowed to a privileged guild of professional
swindlers. Therefore, you, Mr. Gull, must pay
the costs of these proceedings.”
In nine cases out of ten, Mr. Gull goes away
from the court beamingly satisfied, because he
hears that £l6O has been knocked off the bill-of-
costs, and he fancies that he has smitten the
solicitor badly. The solicitor simply smiles
behind his ineffable cheek.
No client has ever yet been mad enough to
suggest to the court that a solicitor, the taxation
of whose costs has virtually branded him as a
swindler, should be sent to gaol for false pretences.

People in general think that the tidal
waves which swell a lawyer’s bill are chiefly the
big fees paid to barristers. Nothing of the kind.
Barristers are fretting evils in their own peculiar
"’ays, but they are mere annoyances compared
with the one big, central, root-grievance.
That is the Office-Boy,
The solicitor’s Office-Boy !
Every time a solicitor’s Office-Boy runs a
message—to carry a note, or post a letter, or look
at the time, or ask when the train starts for
nowhere—somebody is charged 3s. 4d., or 6s. Bd.,
wl3r. 4d., according to the grade of costs in the
particular case.
An Office-Boy, when kept fairly going in this
"my, is as remunerative a property as a big
plumbing business. He is paid from 7s. 6d. to

There is an additional charge of Gs. Bd. per

that professional work is that ho, or a cheap

* # #
Now, scan the brief as a whole.
A brief consists of—(1) the pleadings; (2) the
correspondence, and (in the supposititious instance)
the quoted libel; (3) counsel’s advice. Add to
these the possibility that the solicitor himself may
“ draw ” some statement of the case; sometimes he
does ; sometimes he does not. When he does not,
he simply does nothing at all beyond handing
over the things to be “copied ” to the “tupponce-
a-folio ” man, or to the sweated less-than-a-penny-
a-folio wretch, or to the wages Office-Boy, or to
the premium-paying articled clerk. For, look
you! the barrister “draws” the pleadings; the
correspondence and the newspaper quotations arc
there ready-made; and “counsel’s advice ”is self-
indicative of its origin. The solicitor does
nothing but make out the bill-of costs and pocket
the fees.
Now, a libel-brief such as I have supposed in a
suit against The Bulletin might run to 100
sheets, and there might be three barristers em-
ployed in the cause. In which case, three copies
of the brief would be required, and the entries
alluding to the “ copying’’—and those entries
only, remember, among hundreds of others of
equally ingenious kinds—would appear thus in
the completed bill-of-costs :
The smaller fees for “attending Mr. Limpet”
are reckless generosity on the part of the
solicitor who sends his office-boy, or clerk, with
the brief. It is a sentimental redaction, due only
to the fact that the sum marked on Limpet’s brief
is smaller than those that ornament the briefs of
Shark and Horseleech. It is fantastically
imagined that it is cheaper to climb up five flights
of stairs and wait upon Limpet himself in his sky-
lighted lair than it is to hand the brief to the
office-boys of Shark and Horseleech on the ground
floor. This is perhaps, the only matter in which
a solicitor is humorous at his own expense.
The approximate cost of “copying” 100 sheets
three times over, or 300 sheets, taking it at 6d.
per sheet, would be £7 10s. That, too, would be
at top rates for “copying ” singly each brief three
times over; type-writing by multifold process
would not cost more than .£5. The unhappy
client is, however, charged <£B6 13s. 4d. —a profit
in the one case of <£79 3s. 4d., and in the other of
<£Bl 13s. 4d.
This balance is practically for wear and tear of
the office-boy.
Isn’t the office-boy, rightly used, a solicitor’s
Golconda ?
# # *
Blue foolscap is another mine of wealth to
the solicitor, and greedily drinks the blood of the
man who hires a lawyer.
At every law-stationer’s establishment are to be
purchased one or two hundreds of legal printed
forms, warranted to suit all the diseases of liti-
gants. Each of these various forms takes up half-
a:sheeb of blue foolscap. They are sold at from
Is. to Is. 3d. per dozen—ld. to l|d. apiece. That
price is unblushingly extortionate, but the solicitor
pays it cheerfully, and passes the required docu-
ments on to his client at a charge of several
shillings each—so much for “drawing,” and so
much for “copying.” It is true that the solicitor
does not “draw” anything, while his office-boy,
or articled clerk—or, perhaps, a “ copying” clerk
at 30s. a week ; or, maybe, a sweatee at Id. a folio
—does the “copying.” But it is the legal thing
to charge liberally both for “ drawing ” and
“copying.” Ask the taxing-officer if you don’t
believe me.
Now, every one of these forms that are in legal
use was originally “drawn” by some old cloistered
monk or other in the days of (perhaps) William
the Conqueror, or his son, William, the Jersey-
coloured, and if a solicitor of to-day had the
temerity to alter a single word of their formulae,
exhibited the hardihood of venturing upon any
originality to specially suit the case he had in
hand, the court before whom his audacious
attempt at literature was presented would pro-
bably swoon with surprise, or go off in an in-
dignant fit of apoplexy, or epileptically foam all
over the Judge’s associate.
Yet the client is solemnly and legally rooked
of much substance on the pretence that his lawyer
has been shedding his brain in order to frame a
cunning legal instrument that should exactly fit
into the mazy intricacies of his particular predica-
ment. Whereas, if any solicitor at any time did
draw upon his brain-account for anything original
in the interest of his clients, the items relating to
his brain-perspiration would be ruthlessly cut out
of the bill-of-costs by the taxing-officer.
A solicitor is allowed to charge for “ drawing ”
documents which he never did “draw,” and for
“copying” others which he never did “copy,”
and he is permitted to add about 1200 per cent, on
the cost of everything on which he pays anything.
All these items, the taxing-officer will pass un-
and as a matter of course; but
brain-usage is not scheduled as a fit and proper
item to appear in any bill-of-costs.
In short, our Courts provide no brain allowance,
but recognise a tariff of market rates for
mechanical work. It is a bogus shoddy, humour-
ously and falsely branded “ law,” and it is sold by
the yard—or, rather, by the mile.
• * #
I will hero insert my first moral, my first
note of desired reform
The taxing-officer should not be a lawyer, but
n initiated layman.
# # *
Outside of the taxing jurisdiction the law-
yer lias many fuxinesaes. He is almost invariably
a money-lender, and many loan and discount
agencies and money-lending Hebrew firms have
direct interests in common with many lawyers
concerning whose means of livelihood the public
are often bewildered, on account of their legal
practices being mostly invisible. Money-lending,
however, is such an esoteric branch of the honour-
able profession of the law that I should have to
hire myself out for a term with some Mozzler firm
of “poor people’s friends” before I could authori-
tatively enlarge upon it.
Lawyers shine as debt-collectors. Their common
habit of endeavouring to extort a letter-writing fee
of one guinea, over and above the debt for which
they dun you, is a barefaced, vulgar fraud, which
has been frequently exposed in the columns of
The Bulletin.
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
Seeing Williams.
Melbourne City Police Court is a nice place when
you know it, but you’d got to know it first any
time during business hours on Tuesday, Wednes-
day, and Thursday of last week. Let us suppose
that our entrance to the sanctum was always an
easy matter, unembittered by struggles with an
excited populace on the steps and unimpeded by
vigilant chuckers-out at a succession of narrow
doorways. Enough that we got there, whilst
greater persons, boasting more social distinction,
political influence and stomach, were compelled to
stop outside and snort. Magistrates, solicitors,
Ms.L. A., and all sorts of pillars of the State
pleaded for admission and “ took the numbers”of
the inflexible constabulary without making the
least difference. Even a sublime flunkey from the
Legislative Council couldn’t reach the court
until he had frothed at the mouth and called
Heaven to witness that sundry policemen didn’t
address him as “sir.” Nobody called vs “sir,”
but we swallowed our indignation and said
nothing.
* * »
The interior of this court, also of most other
courts, suggests a private chapel where people
don’t go to pray, or a small theatre in which
encores are prohibited. On the present occasion
it is like a chapel licensed for a theatrical per-
formance, but devoid of an orchestra. Let us
make for the ground floor, or stalls, entrance to
the sacred shovvplace, and obtain admission at the
door on the right. This is the nearest way of
getting close to the Immortal Williams. Wil-
liams is not held up to admiration as he might be.
He occupies a chair in the right-hand aisle of the
court, at the end of the solicitors’ table, and he
sits lower than the rest of the congregation. This
is a sad mistake. Directly the show opens George
Rignold (supporting his old friend Dr. Neild on
the bench) wonders what in the name of blank the
stage-manager is about. Why not place the “star”
miscreant upon a scaffolding and turn on the lime-
light? The same thought occurs to J. C. Williamson
and Teddy Royce when they look in next morning,
and Billy Elton, away up in the gallery, considers
that a low comedian of Williams’ versatility is
wasted by not being put on the table beside the
canary-cage. On the first day, however, Williams
is frequently ordered to stand up and be sworn to,
a ceremony which pleases the little man for awhile,
because he is glad of an opportunity to stretch his
legs and smile at the j ury on the cross bench near
the dooi’, on the opposite side of the court. But
the novelty of bobbing up soon wears off, and he
gets sick of being disturbed. He can lean back
in his chair and paralyse the fair sex in the left-
hand gallery with an occasional glance of his
hard, magnetic, reddish - grey eye. A cold,
hot, amorous unsympathetic eye, that gleams
from under a bunch of light-carrotty eyebrows
protruding from a well-marked bump of percep-
tion. The maids and matrons in the right-hand
gallery can’t feel the influence of this icy, burning
optic, but they enjoy a fine view of the gentle-
man’s head, which, despite a nasty slope back
from the brows, would be a possible casket for
many virtues. Some of the jury carry heads less
prepossessing. Venus and Adonis! how the
women do gloat on this puny, round-shouldered
plumber, with the abnormal chin. He is notorious,
and notoriety is more dazzling than diamonds.
Williams, an expert in female human nature of
the middle and lower grades, knows that the
tallowy widow in the front row would take
him to her flabby bosom (if nobody were
looking) as eagerly as those three spinstei-s who
hang over the balcony a few feet nearer to the
coroner. His high spirits are excusable. No
wonder that he looks with rollicking derision at
Marshall Lyle, his solicitor. Lyle is a compara-
tively lovely creature, with curly hair trained
down to a point in the centre of his milk-white
forehead, and a long, brown, Scriptural kind of
beard fringing his fair young face, yet no heart in
court palpitates for him .
• • *
It is not to be supposed that every woman
coming to see Williams is necessarily the slave of
his celebrity. Gracie Whiteford, who secures a
good dress-circle seat next to Widow O’Brien
Sheridan, takes a purely professional view of the
case. Williams stands about sft. 3iin., has a
shapely, aquiline nose, a mobile face, and a magni-
ficent sense of humour. “He’d make up very
well for the Widow,” remarks Gracie, and the
x’eal Mrs. O’Brien endorses this opinion. But it
would take them all their time to make up a
suitable expression for Williams’ mouth, which
has a nasty trick of opening like an iron rat-trap
and closing with a jerk.
* * *
Perhaps, too, those lady “ journalists ” who
invade the floor of the Court on the first day, and
flap their huge note-books with ostentatious pride,
are guiltless of any devouring passion for
Williams. One hopes so, because Williams is so
recklessly indifferent to their charms. When
at last the rustling mob begin to close
round his chair, and stare up his nostrils
and down his hairy ears, the little hero
gnashes his rat-trap at them, as many a
better man would under like provocation. Solo-
mons, the dealer, has a real grievance against
these appalling daughters of tea-party literature,
since it was they who infuriated the hearthstone
man and caused him to land poor Solly one in the
eye as he was being bustled out of court to his
luncheon in the corridor adjoining the cells hard
by. It was here that Williams liked to have a
snack, all calm and peaceful-like amongst the
constables. He used quite bad language to a
young artist assisting George Ashton in work for
a London paper, who attempted a sketch of the
hyena at feeding-time. Later on he aimed a
potato through a small glass window at the same
artist, but probably this was hurled in play.
Williams is as full of play as a barrel-organ
grinder.
« # #
The animosity which Williams cherishes for
Mr. Solomons is not explained by their previous
relations. The Hebrew dealer has done no harm
to the other man, although lie might have gone a
trifle higher than three “quid ” for the histoin
canary. Witnesses walk into the box, one after
another, and identify “the man we call Williams
at the end of the table,” with events much more
damning than the incidental sale of the caged bird
that sings so sweetly on the table there in front of
Prosecutor Walsh, Q.C. Walsh doesn’t sing
sweetly—but we digress. Williams seems to bear
no particular grudge against these other people.
His disliking for Solomons is as the unaccountable
antipathy of a dog to a particular visitor at his
master’s house —the passion of a brute beast. It
is not to be reconciled with the man’s evident
liking for Max Hirschfeldt, who went over to
Western Australia with the detectives to identify
his late shipmate. Max treats Williams as a
capital joke, and Williams leers quite tenderly at
the trim German gentleman as he repeats in the
witness-box his expression of ojnnion that prisoner
“will hang as sure as God made little apples.”
They are bound together by a merry bond of
humour. N.B— Did God make little Williams ?
* • •
Williams grows shockingly funny as the case
goes on, and the sergeants and constables in
attendance, smart, bright fellows one and all, as
it happens, are constantly ; railing at the mad
wag. They will miss him when he’s hanged, no
doubt. All evidence that bears upon the cement
and hearthstone episode strikes Williams as be’ng
extra droll. His profile at these times gives one
the impression that he is about to oblige the com-
pany with a comic song. Some of Coroner Neild’s
comrades on the bench permit the dim twilight of
a grin to steal across their faces now and again,
although for the most part (these distinguished
patrons of the drama come and go frequently)
they are dull, pompous individuals. Harrie
Smith, a racing-club secretary closely related to
some other Smith of legal standing, expands
his cheeks under the mahogany canopy for
a time on the second day. Panton, P.M.,
comes in through the magistrates’ door
at the back and gazes serenely on his
court. Sylvester Biowne, the silver king, hand-
some son of Auak and brother to “ Rolf Boldre-
wood,” stands within the enclosure, to the coroner’s
left, and tries his hand at sketching Williams. He
is unobserved of the subject, for Williams at tlvs
time is trying to mash a Sydney lady, the only
“ bit of muslin ” downstairs, who occupies a seat
beside old Townsend Macdermott, the barrister,
under the shadow of the Bench. Macdermott,
having himself failed to catch the eye of beauty,
shifts Ins seat, and betrays his jealousy by scowl-
ing at Williams worse than ever. Next day, when
Williams falls asleep in court, and loses himself in
the land of happy dreams, the unfeeling Mac.
swears that his successful rival is a wide-awake
impostor. “ Why didn’t he fall asleep yesterday?”
growls Macdermott, doubtless thinking of the
angel with maize ribbons on her hat.
# # *
Kate Rounsevcll comes on towards the end of
the second act. She had flitted across the stage,
so to speak, in the first act for the purpose of
being recognised by an ancient mariner, but that
was nothing. All the rest of the time up to now
Kate has been at the wings—meaning the ante-
room or vestry behind the witness-box—waiting
for her cue to come forward and knock ’em. The
Hirschfeldts are in charge of the leading lady, and
Williams (since he learned this fact) has vainly
endeavoured to breathe his vows anew through
the medium of genial Max. Enter Miss Rounse-
vell. Young reporters, scribbling away in the
centre pews, or reserved stalls, are disposed to
over-estimate the charms of Miss Kate, who is in
truth a very passable vision in a saucy hat and a
dark-blue cream-fronted dress, cut to give her
slight figure a good chance. Emotion is not the
damsel’s forte, and she never gets xvithin
measurable distance of a flood of tears,
or anything of that sort. Her collected man-
ner in the witness-box is in harmony with the
story she tells of a strange “gentleman,” diamond
rings, a lightning courtship, an intended marriage,
and a shattered dream —all within a month. Miss
Kate seems to bear her loss with Christian resigna-
tion. Girls, as a rule, are proud of the man they
mean to marry. One would be interested to learn
whether this placid young spinster, after a hasty
glance at “ Baron Swanston ” in his present state,
recognises the influence of diamonds and mu u;•
tachios in the most important events of this life.
* * *
It is hard to understand that Williams was ever
a gaudy, boastful man. One can imagine him
with a swag, but not with a swagger. Since his
arrest he has at once aged in appearance and yet
gone back in his style, until he has indeed become
the type of an elderly, jocular, lustful plumber
with a natural turn for ferocity. We are all so
wise, you see, after the event; we see so many
virtues in the well-dressed, successful man; so
many villainies in the fellow behind the spikes.
« * *
Williams undoubtedly frets—hungrily, savagely,
humorously over the loss of Miss Rounsevell.
His dark, auburn-brown hair would glitter With
electric sparks if she came into his cell and smiled
upon him. Dr. Neild and his medical brethren
who line the Bench on the last day agree in think-
ing that Williams would choke Kate if she ven-
tured within arm’s-reach of her baffled Lothario.
No. He might bite a piece off Kate, but not for the
joy of injuring her. Merely for the pleasure of
chewing the delicate morsel, and carrying the
flavour of it to his grave. The mad sensualist
takes precedence of the brutal murderer in Mr.
Williams-Deeming. Probably he has always been
a kind, pleasant, well-meaning man during the
first two or three days of his honeymoons. With
the original Mrs. D. he was not a bad sort of
fellow for several years. She suited him much
better than pious Emily Mather suited him.
Besides, his disease was in its infancy when he
lived in Sydney.
* * «
Giddy barmaids in gay apparel decorated the
opposite gallery on the third day and ogled
Williams to his heart’s content whilst the jury
were drawing up an elaborate verdict. His nap
had refreshed him, and his demeanour was
riotously amorous. The show was drawing to a
close, he would be shut off from any view of
millinery very soon, so he must feast his eyes on
lovely woman ere the curtain fell. He went out
in high glee, inflamed by the melodious “Oh-o-o!”
of the ladies when he advised Coroner Neild to
“put the verdict in his pipe and smoke it.” There
was a damsel near the gangway as Williams was
being rushed away to the cells. Rapture!
Williams made a sudden dash, managed to chuck
her under the chin, and murmured, “You
duckey !” Truly a characteristic exit.
“ There was no furniture in the house
except an old whitewash-brush and a piece of
cardboard,” said Sergeant o‘Longhlen at the
Deeming inquest. Which reminds us very much
of the man who had no clothes on except an oil-
painting and a stove. 1

SIC Macquarie-street North, Sydney, r April 13, 1892.
My Dear Aloorarinda, —
People who like to draw distinctions betxveen the sexes
flattering to the male-half of humanity ought (making all
due alloxvance for the difference between the manners of
the haul ton and the bourgeoisie) to find substance for
much moralising in the contrast of Capt. Osborne’s
behaviour to his guilt}’ xvife over the Hargreaves pearl-
stealing case and the way in which Aliis Rounsevell
rounded on her ex-adorer Deeming and gax e him away,
lock, stock and barrel, at the first hint of anything un-
pleasant. Capt. Osborne—as everybody knows—stood
beside the dock containing his wretched wife during the
whole trial; Aliss Rounsevell seemingly agreed with the
first person who pronounced against her lover—and as for
lending him personal support! well, if she had, mind you,
people would have sneered olex erly at the strange blind-
ness of every woman to a possible husband’s villainies—
but all the same, if she had stuck to him and, publicly at
least, refused to believe guilty until ho was proved guilty
the man whom she had set out to marry, she would have
cut a much better figure in the dreadful affair. And
what a valuable wide-world advertisement there was in it!
She could have posed for weeks in print as the embodi-
ment of faithfulness, trust, true, unquestioning love and
ail the rest of the dog-like virtues which man hankers
after in a wife ; and to be practical, xvho knows what
wealthy offers of marriage she might have secured by-
reason of said virtues after they bad been judiciously
advertised in a thousand ncxvspapers? Instead she refused
even to grant him an interview' (I hear she feared he
might injure her in self-defence), and wound up by testify-
ing against him at the inquest with a bunch of flowers in
lier jacket!
No matter how criminal the man may seem, a woman
who, in his palmy days, professed enough love for him to
set out for a distant part of Australia to be ids xvife, ought
not to come gaily adorned into court among his accusers.
However much “ her love had turned to loathing” com-
mon decency gave her a part to act and she should have
acted it. But the lady’s sudden enmity to her alleged
1n er is, after all, surely only a public expression of the
sentiment that would forever have remained underneath,
had 'Deeming been the rich man he swaggered to be.
Wonvn hate the very name of the Decming-Ronnsvell
affair because it is such a complete revelation of the
heights and depths of the mercenary or cupboard-love
side of their character. Not, please note, that I think my
poor, dear sex are to blame, in any way for connubializing
with a banking-account—it is entirely the fault of
their training, and so I r.o more think of condemning a
woman for breaking her neck after a man who can write
big cheques than 1 xvould whip a monkey for dancing on
a barrel-organ. And even if a woman is brought up to
judge for herself, social conditions soon make it cruelly
plain that money is the lover for her. Every level-headed
woman sets a reserve price on herself, as it were—the
foolish virgins do the other thing, with the result that,
in a fexv years, the foolish ex-virgin’s pa finds himself
supporting bis daughter, her husband, and a swarm of
infants with power to add to their number. If there is no
solid relative to sponge on, the valiant girl xvho heroically
casts in her lot with one pound per week, is pawning her
boots before sbe knows where she is, and helping to get an
Act of Parliament passed to have a new orphan asylum
erected—so you can hardly' blame the girl who is bowled
out in a dollar-chase, can you? In the long run, the
mercenary woman, even if she never limes her bird, is far
less expense and worry to the community than the trust-
ing idiot who'gives-her whole soul into the fond keeping
of the first eligible suitor with a throbbing heart and
paper collar, who chances to like the colour of her hair.
The psychological similarity of one woman ti another,
no matter what colour she is or what language she clothes
her ideas in, xvas again exemplified—in Melbourne this
time—on the first day of Alister Deeming’s trial. Said a
German lady-witness Mr. and Airs. Williams travelled
with us on the Kaiser Wilhelm, and I xvas sure Airs.
Williams’ upper teeth were false !” The remark had to
lie interpreted from German to English, by the way, but
how familiar it must have sounded to every man in court!

It is Impossible to carp at the altogether lovely staging
accorded to “Master and Man” at Sydney Royal; the
only little rifts I discovered in the show were that Robert
Carlton’s housemaid officiated in a white gown and car-
dinal sleeves instead of in the sombre black that should
distinguish the female menial; and that Robert Carlton
himself ought to wear both gloves when the spirit moves
him to conceal his hands, instead of putting on only the
left and leaving the right dangling, after the fashion of
ten years ago. Otherwise the staging was more complete
than real life, for in every-day existence when you reach
out in a hurry after anything it isn’t there, hut Bland
Holt’s characters have everything right to their hands
without stretching even. Mrs. Bland Holt, as larky
be tty Lighlfoot, sported three Dresden Chinaish cos-
tumes—in the first act, a cream delaine designed in red
Cherries and looped over a cream petticoat, Dolly garden
hat with cherries all over it; Kate Bishop at the same
time looking particularly pretty in a heliotrope cashmere
with cascade of mulberry ribbons. In Act 2 Miss Bishop
wore sage-green, and Mrs. Bland Holt pale primrose
muslin with floral design in crimson, the front made A la
pinafore, a crimson sash passing underneath the drapery,
which fell straight from the throat—a gorgeous design,
by the way, for stout figures. A soft, blue-grey gown
with “cross-over” of pale pink silk with flounced edges
was Mrs Holt’s final thrill; but Miss Bishop (who by that
time had reached the very bed-rock of artistically-sug-
gested grief) had to content with a disconsolate tweed
garment blacker than her shadow.
Her Majesty’s Theatre Stock Co. bade Sydney adieu for
a lengthy period the other Friday, finishing up nobly
with “Nowadays.” Miss Watts-Phillips played Jenny
Dowling with her usual success, and I liked her best in
the heliotrope corduroy gown with vest of painted satin
and trimmings of silver lace, hat with wreath of myrtle,
which she sported in the last scene. There was a good
house to see the (professionally) last of the company, who
left for Adelaide on 9th inst. A great many friends
assembled to send them off, and Miss Watts-Phillips was
the recipient, amongst other parting offerings, of an
exquisite floral horse-shoe of white and pink roses,
cosmea, &c.
“ Martha” was revived at the Garrick, the other night,
with much riotous vocalism, but the only thing I saw to
interest me was that Cavalliera had painted herself red in
several places. Youth and beauty do not assume the form
of a crushed-strawberry blush rising like an Aurora from
the spine of the party so far as I - after a long experience
silting behind the V-shaped d.c. darling—am able to
judge ; but whenever an actress begins daubing herself
in the no-thoroughfares of her person with Chinese rouge,
it’s safe to bet that she means to look like seventeen from
the front. You can always tell the intentional juvenile
of the stage by her aspect of being brilliantly picked out
with carmine wherever she shows her clothing. Vocally,
I-like Cuttica least of all in “Martha,” but that is the
fault of the soprano music, which is peppered with tram-
signal head-notes as if the heroine wants to warn you that
she is coming round the corner. The interpolated “ Last
Rose of Summer” scored an encore and good flowers,
two excited females in a box recklessly pitching down
two corsage-sprays of autumn leaves; but some of Mrs.
C-’s business, in the semi-duet, looked as if she was trying
to exorcise Cuttica after approved spiritualistic methods.
One tiling she deserves praise for is the way she manages
the tail of her gown, which is always humbly and pro-
perly flowing behind -but I can’t say the same of
Cavalliera. It really doesn’t look the thing for an artiste
to retreat up stage with her train hanging out like a
tongue between her teeth, does it? There was a very
good house on this occasion, but the only moral to be
deduced from the d.c. was that, when you are sailing
under a most unmistakeable false front it is one of the
silliest things in the world to entangle an attractive
ornament in your borrowed bang.

their stay in Hobart. His Lordship graciously played (bo
organ, and “ Lady Leamington ” sang a solo. The
crowded congregation—apprised beforehand of the in-

The distinguished visitors were afterwards entertained by
the delighted parson. Alas, the joy of the shepherd

charge of child-desertion made against him in Sydney
will be abandoned in favour of a more serious accusation.

On Saturday night Bland Holt’s bull-dug,
supported by an attractive company, opened at
Sydney Royal for a short season of “ Master and
Man.” Also, he remained open most of the time
the show was in progress—in fact, we have never
before seen an animal that is so seldom shut—and
selected small bits of refreshment off the'various
bad characters of the story at short intervals.
Barring the dog, none of the male characters in
this drama will ever be murdered in cold blood
for their moral excellence. In strict accordance
with the laws of melodrama the worst individual
in the whole category wears a pointed moustache,
and the best one is clean-shaved. “ Master and
Man ” was written specially for Mr. Bland Holt’s
bull-dog; and its ostensible motive is the fact
that a sweet and lovely girl, who lives in a
cottage with a bill-of-sale upon it, is really the
heiress to a great estate, but is kept out of it
owing to her mother’s marriage being a secret
from everybody except the malignant hunchback
who travels on two withered legs gone askew and
has horrid dreams. The girl herself has no idea of
her identity, and would never, in the ordinary
course of events, become aware of it, but that her
enemy is crushed by the unsupported assertion of
the funny man in the last act and by an affidavit
attested by a lunatic who has burst out of his
asylum. These facts are put in to draw attention
off the bull-dog so that he may not dawn upon the
pmblicjtoo suddenly. All through the three prin-
cipal acts the present owner of the property
harrows up the feelings of the rightful heiress by
remorseless persecution, and embezzles her child,
and runs her husband into gaol, and tries to sell
up her aunt, and nearly harasses her off her
mental basis by starting the hunchback after her
at all times and seasons, and but for the fact that
the bull-dog takes up her cause manfully and that
she has the moral support of the funny man, she
would sink under her sufferings. But just when
things are at their worst her husband escapes from
gaol; and a party of workmen try to shove the
hunchback head-first into a furnace, so that lie
repents, and becomes mad, and goes round in a
partially-boiled condition to undo bis past evil
deeds ; and the blown who stole the child has
his eye blackened in front of a public-house ; and
Bland Holt and the bull-dog rush in excitedly
with some facts which they assert they picked up
in the pocket of a person who was drunk, and
which explain everything; and the dog has to
hold himself down by the tail with superhuman
vigour, else he would leap upon the baffled villain
and eat him raw, and everybody, save the wrong-
ful owner of the property, is happy. He isn’t
happy, but he tries to assume a jaunty aspect in
the hour of ruin, only lie feels that the dog’s eye
is upon him and breaks up miserably.
* # «
The bull-dog in this gorgeous drama is
played by Beelzebub, and bis performance
throughout is beyond all praise. He fills his part
most of the time with a quiet intensity that is
very striking, but at critical moments he rises to
an impassioned fervour such as few actors can
attain. Yet he is never guilty of rant or undue
straining after effect. His part, unfortunately, is
hardly large enough to give full scope for his
talents, but be is distinctly a dog that will yet
rise to the highest rank in the profession. In
such a character as that of Othello he would
undoubtedly be a great success, and several
Othellos we have seen might take Ids part as the
bull-pup with advantage. He is ably supported
by Miss Kate Bishop and Mrs. Bland Holt, two
ladies of whom no Australian audience ever gets
weary. Bland Holt himself takes the part of
the funny-man aforementioned, and is one of
the few funny men who are funny, the
average funny-man of melodrama being as solemn
as Cain after the murder of Abel, and having
“ Ichabod ” written large all over them, only
hidden by their clothes. Also, Charles Glenny is
a picturesque and able character as the malignant
hunchback who potters along in the heroine’s
wake, and W. E. Baker makes a conspicuous
success in one of the minor characters. The rest
of the company are good, as a whole, but they are
overshadowed by the dog, and would be seen to
better advantage if associated with an animal of
smaller call In 0. Only an actor of great natural
ability can live up to a dog of the first order —in
fact most men can't even live up to a mongrel.
ct despite some small drawbacks “Master and
Man ” is an assured success, and the scenery, as
nsu>l, is magnificent and is laid on with a lavish
hand. Unit ss the bull-dog is confronted by some
altogether unexpected c'rcumstanees, the pro-
gramme will undergo no change till the 23rd.
* * * *
“ Caste is still on view at Sydney
Criterion, and Anson, in his capacity as Eccles, is
still engaged in reluctantly tearing himself away
horn tlio scene of his ancient glories. But we
begin to have doubts about bis going after all. He
has played Eccles s-> long that the passions of a
crunk desire for gin and the baleful influence of a
base yearning to put his baby grandson’s caul up
the spout and drink the proceeds, seem likely to
eat into his system ami paralyse him. Therefore
there appeals to be a fair chance that he will stay
on and settle down permanently on the Criterion
stage as the unshaven Eccles, and that
“Caste ’ will go m for ever like a hypnotised
comedy ( r a fixed automaton in three acts,
and the advertisement stating that Anson is about
to quit will take root in the daily papers and stay
there till the old age of the earth. If Brough
and Boueicault desire to have a long rest from the
weariness, of scene-shifting and rehearsals, they
can't do better than engage the present company
for life, and let “Caste” go straight ahead as
long as the scenery will hold together. And when
the last performance comes along, and Brough
and Lyndal both have to play their parts on
crutches, and the infant is 40 years of age, and the
fair young mother is white-haired, and wears
spectacles, and Eccles, owing to extreme feeble-
ness, has to make ins entrances and exits on all
fours oven then “Caste” would be a paying
speculation if the Methusaleh in the office wasn’t
too (>ld and broken-up to keep the accounts
straight.

“ The School for Scandal,” written by a
dead, bottle-nosed gentleman whose name is not
mentioned on the bill, has superseded “ Masks
and Faces” at Melbourne Opera House. Hie
reason for this change is not strikingly evidnit.
There was good business for ‘ Masks and Faces,
in which Mrs. Beere and her chief adherents shone
with no uncertain light, whereas the other old
comedy doesn’t display them to great advantage.
Moreover, the whole performance suffers by com-
parison with the Brough Boueicault effort of three
or four years ago, and tends to lower the prestige
of the new company. Royce and Cat heart, both
of honoured Bijou memory, are better .suited to
their parts—Uncle Oliver and Peter Teazle—than
any of the others. Young Otho Stuart, as Joseph
Surface, is not up to good amateur form, and
his face (which, under a powdered wig i*
a facsimile of Mrs. Brough’s) ridicules the
bare notion of Ids being elder brother
to such a mature reveller as Herbert Standing.
The latter actor, reviewed far apart from I"*
alleged relative, is a very acceptable Char es
Surface, and seems to have fixed himself in public
favour, anyway. Mrs. Bernard Deere contributes
a stroke of genius at the end of the famous screen
scene, but for the most part her Lady Teazle i*
not an especially fa'cinating-or convincing piJna
of the clever country girl who finishes her educa-
tion in a bad London “ School.” She is hard an'
formal throughout the giddy interview wi
Joseph, and the cool, calculating way in w | lK '
she discusses the advisability of being led astra.
by that verdant shepherd is inconsistent with 1
subsequent burst of penitence. Marius pois'd
pleasantly as Trip, also Emery and Hans 1 lip JE
are capable scandalmongers, but the subordina
ladies fall short of what is expected of
historic characters. “ The School for Scanda.
however, will break up this (Thursday) evein g;

Mr. Acting-Justice Heydon’s president-
ship of Sydney Criminal Court during the week,
has, on the whole, proved a great success. But
why did His Honor send Richardson up for four
years for putting his signature to a fraudulent
balance-sheet and give Kirby, who cut Ins mother's
throat, only two years? Was it because Richard-
son cooked his accounts whereas Kirby only half-
cooked his ma ?
The Chief-Justice of Victoria does not
mince matters when he speaks of the manner in
which the papers have prejudged Williams, and
should he try the case his charge to the jury will
probably embody some acrid comments on the
subject of press prosecutions. Dr. Mannington
Caffyn appears to have been entrusted with the
superintendence of the medical aspect of Williams’
defence, and there is a certainty of a big rumpus
in the medical world ere his trial comes on. The
authorities decline, as in Colston’s case, to allow
any medical witness for the defence to see the
prisoner without the presence of the Government
medical-officer, the plea being that it is likely
that the gentleman so acting might suggest to the
accused methods of dissimulation—truly a deli-
ciously complimentary estimate of the profes-
sion. There can, it is thought, be no
effectual effort to save the prisoner’s life. The
only hope of the defence probably is to prove his
criminal lunacy. Nothing has astonished indepen-
dent observers who have come into contact with
the man so much as his absolute stupidity. Wil-
liams mentally is essentially a fool, without even
the ordinary cunning of the criminal class; his
success with his late cemented wives has been due
more to luck and sheer carelessness than to any
deliberate planning. He belongs to the order of
instinctive criminals whose moral sense is as
wanting as a blind man’s sight. By the way, the
moral sense of the court full of gaping sightseers
was much more shocked when Dr. Mullen
announced that he had tried the weapon supposed
to have been used by Williams on the skull of a
dead woman than at any other incident of the
trial.

Tenor Boyle, who is chiefly remembered by his singing
of the “ blue blood ” air in the early Australian produc-
lions of “ Inlanlhe,” died in England the other day. He
had a sweet voice, but he was a hopelessly bad actor, and
drank like a fish.

A testimonial to the veteran Andrew Torning, of
Sydney, is proposed. In the forties and fifties, Madame
Torning was Australia’s premiere danseuso ; her husband
then did good work as an actor, and was locally unrivalled
as a scene-painter.
A well-dressed party, among which was a girl of re-
nowned beauty, with a "large part of that renowned beauty
shining, unconcealed, beneath the electric-light, occupied
a parterre box in a N.Y. theatre. A heavy, red-faced
young fellow, who arrived late after a hearty dinner,
plunged into the box and stood perfectly motionless
gazing at the fine back of the beauty, who was unaware
of Ills presence. After a moment lie hastily drew a card-
case from his pocket, extracted a postage-stamp (here-
from, and, moistening ttie stamp, leaned forward and
pressed it between the girl’s shoulders. Everyone in the
box saw the strange action, and the girl herself turned
with flushed face to see who had taken such a liberty with
iter. The young man looked fiercely into iter eyes.
“You’re all right now,” lie muttered. “I’ve stamped
you, and you’ll go.” “Go?” echoed the girl. “Go
where ?” “To it by male.”—Ex.

More than once have we protested against the con-
certed trick of the Melbourne theatrical critics refraining
to mention when an artist is laid aside by illness. As, for
instance, in “ Dorothy,” habituds have been deluded witli
the idea that they were going to see Marie Halton and
Ryley, whereas they only saw Pollard and Deane. Within
a few months the critical senility to managers has been
similarly proved, in Melbourne, with regard to Billce
Barlow, Rignold, Nelly Barren, and Danby.
The Palmer dramatic company, which is doing a season
on the west coast of Maoriland, appears to have got there
under difficulties. They were weatherbound among the
Alps, sundry landslides blocked the road, and they camped
out under a deluge of rain and other horrors. The
villain and the heavy father had to dig tip the principal
heroine, who was bogged ; and the walking gentleman
nearly broke his back fishing the soaked infant pheno-
menon out of a mountain torrent. At latest they had
reached Beefton.
The hotel-keepers of St. Louis (IT.S.A) have struck
against Sarah Bernhardt. That lady readied their city
accompanied by six dogs, three snakes, and eleven birds,
and as no one would take in her menagerie she camped in
a palace car at the railway station.

Th e Wild-Cat Column.
A new “ salting ” yarn. A. was a well-known
salter, who had a claim on a Northern field. He told
Messrs. 8., Cl and D. that he had “struck it,” and offered
to sell; he would let them test the claim in his absence.
B. went below to shoved up the dirt; C. worked the
windlass, and i>. panned out the stuff, while A. got drunk
in a township two miles away. Yet they were badly
“ had,” for halt-way down the shaft, a small chamber had
been dug behind the timbering, and A.’s youthful heir,
about 10, salted each bucket as it passed him on its road
up and down.

When the brokers themselves have no money,
stand from under. To'offer 1000 shares for sale of any
stock is to produce an avalanche. The bulk of the Sydney
brokers arc jobbers and many of these are penniless
people, to deal with whom is to act on the principle of
heads they win, tails you lose.
* * #
A newspaper which arrogates to itself wonderful
prescience when it happens to come out right in its
weekly mining market guesses, and is as silent as the grave
on its own blunders in other directions, says of the
Bonanza Consols that “ when once it strikes a good pay-
able lode the shares will take a jump ! ” Wonderful, but
true. Less wonderful but equally true that when once
one of the great number of enraged individuals who
purchased thousands of these shares (now worth two-
pence) at from Bs. to 12s. on the advice of the same
tipster strikes, something else will take a jump, too.
» # *
It is almost impossible to work a big dividend-
paying mine without throwing fortunes in the way of the
management. In this respect the big mines of Broken
Hill are the most extravagant in Australia. Baton made
more money by purchasing and selling shares at the right
moment than by his enormous salary. One of the highest
buildings in Mtlbourne is the substantial result of a
Broken Hill tip. The tipster made a fortune for himself
and fox many of his friends, who have since sworn by his
judgment. We should think so—to buy Centrals, Souths,
and Block Tens at shillings and sell at as many pounds
was certainly good business.

“There is a complete plant, including battery of
ten head, with stock of quicksilver, acids, copperplates,
blacksmith’s shop, sheds and accessories, mine, mining
tools, and I will take £lOO for the lot/’ said, a few days
ago, a disconsolate man whose mining enthusiasm had
busted.
• # #
The next mining boom will be in gold. Booms,
like mushrooms, are forced and short-lived. The gold-
boom will be forced by the world’s scarcity, and conse-
quent hunger for that metal. It will also be short-lived,
because no matter how many rich gold-discoveries may
possibly be made, people will go mad, and scarcity of the
metal which gave the boom birth will also cause its
death.

Zeehan were quoted(Queens, £7 to £8; Adelaide
props., £7 to £8; Comet, 225. to 195.; Hobarfes, £45 to
£5O ; Hassett’s, 4s. to 55.; Anderson, 2s. Gd. to 35.; Aus-

Gd. to Gs. Gd.; Ilassett’s, 4d. to 5d.; Andersons, Cd.; Aus-
tral, Is. to Is. Cd.; Melbourne props., sd. to Gd.; Kozinin-
sky’s. Is. Gd.; Balstrups, 4s. Cd. to 55.; Silver King, 425.;
whilst dozens of others selling at big figures a year ago
(companies of 100,000 and 50,000 shares) are at the present
time unheard of; in fact, those who are “minding the
baby" find it difficult to obtain any information about
them, nor can they find out where the sections are
situated. They had then hundreds of feet of “basks”
available without the aid of machinery and very little
money required. To-day there are no backs, no machinery,
no money.
» # *
Things seem about a toss-up at Zeehan, and
the future chiefly depends on the bottoming of a couple
of shafts, namely, Central Dundas and Comet, and these
with Maestries are big prospective shows and will com-
paratively make or break the field. If silver-bearing
lodes on the Barrier were a criterion the success of this
place would be assured, but they differ in every respect;
there are numerous lead-lodes at Zeehan and Dundas, but
the extraction of gangue such as zinc blend, carbonate of
iron, &0., will yet cause some terrible trouble, and, so far,
with hardly an exception, at a depth the lodes begin to
mix with base minerals. The Government have bad all
the best of it so far, extracting all the revenue they can
lay hands upon for no return. They have recently passed
a special measure for the extraction cf about £16,000 per
month for six months from Residence Areas, just at a
time when the place is fairly poverty-stricken. The
people are waking up and threaten to dynamite the whole
island rather than submit.
• * *
A cute American, who has been in turn a
temperance-evangelist, insurance-agent, and, finally, a
mining-thrillist, and all with varying financial success,
has gone back to the evangelist-temperance business. It
is said of him that he recently received an “ awakening of
the spirit.” The writer is speculating whether the spirit
would have awakened him had his silver-quartz lodes not
pinched out instead of going down, as he eloquently pre-
dicted, a thousand feet.
# » * #
Three men had recently a Sydney bulk crushing
—a ton—and they refused to be consoled with the result,
which, to several persons not in the “know,” was emi-
nently satisfactory. They persisted in asking why this
disgust and dissatisfaction. “Well, you see, it was this
way,” said one of the too candid miners “we put twice
as much in the confounded stuff.” The machine was
responsible for the difference.
* * #
History, in brief, of many N.S.W. mines, born,
nursed, and dead, within the last three years. Booms
come in cycles—the bigger the boom the longer the
interval before the next. Hill End was a big boom fol-
lowed by a long cessation. The last was started by
Broken 11111. It is the easiest tiling in the world to find
silver and gold in infinitesimal quantities in any mineral
country, and as time will not allow waiting for something
good to be found, the first thing is taken. The assay of
selected samples is good, the expert’s report is better,
lying does the rest. A company is floated, a working
miner with an elasticconscience gets a billet as manager.
It is his duty tq spend money and write encouraging
reports, and he does it A few shareholders who display
even the judgment of the mule get out and receive the
sympathy of those who stay in. Calls become mono-
tonous. At last the machinery is ready and there is an
abundance of “good-looking rock at grass.” For many
unaccountable reasons crushing is delayed. At last the
day arrives and several directors go up to bring down the
cake, sometimes arranging for a police-escort to the rail-
way-station. Several hundred ounces expected, less than
fifty received. Escort discharged, the result revealed and
excused in many ways. Next crushing worse ; shares
forfeited in thousands. Meeting to consider financial
position ; another to wind-up. Liquidator appointed.
Plant, which cost £3OOO, and mine sold for £3OO.
* * *
Every penny fall in the price of silver makes a
difference of £50,000 a year to the Broken Hill Pro-
prietary mine.
* * *
Mining knowledge combined with sound judg-
ment of intrinsic value made fortunes for managers of
the Barrier, who are now left, high and dry because they
could not foresee the turn of the market tide which a
year ago they proudly imagined would carry them up to
Patou’s financial level.
# # *
Two men working together a mine and a market,
one shall be taken and the other left. Of course the man
who made money by going out is a swindler. Those who
remain in always try to kick him instead of kicking
themselves.

A telegram from Mount Stewart says : —“ Big
smelter working splendidly ; bullion assaying 140 oz. to
the ton—nine tons despatched.” Again we say this is
absolutely useless information. Why not say how many
tons of ore one ton of bullion represents, so that the
grade of the ore may be ascertained? It is all in tlie silver
and lead contents of the ore, not of the bullion, which signi-
fies nothing but ifidefinitencss, of which, after many
warnings, the poor public are sick unto death.

warmth is necessary for the preservation of
health for young and old. By sending an order
to Finney, Isles and Co., Brisbane, with a remit-
tance to cover amount, a pair of excellent, soft,
white Blankets will be sent by return parcel post
to any address in the colony. Fine large size for
single beds, with postage included, 12/8; extra
quality, 15/3 per pair ; superior large, for three-
quarter beds, 15/9 and 18/9 ; superfine, for double
beds, 19/6 and 24/-; extra super., largest size for
double beds, with plenty to tuck in all round,
23/6, 30/- and 35/-; superior quality, state bed
size, 40/-, 50/- and 60/- per pair. Residents in the
country, by comparing the fine, heat-givingquaht.V
and value of Finney, Isles and .Co.’s Blankets with
the poor quality usually sold at the same or higher
prices, will find the result to be as astonishing as
satisfactory. Poor quality will be easily detected
by the raw, unfinished surface of the inside.
Customers living outside the radius of the Parcels
Post delivery should avail themselves of it to the
nearest point it reaches, where they could rend
for their parcels.
