Personal Items.
“Money ” Miller’s widow died a few days
back.
Speight will probably demand £lO,OOO for
the Age alleged libel.
“ John Williams,” a reporter says, is the
favourite Police-court false name.
Oscar Wilde was originally christened, not
“Oscar,” but “John O’Flaherty” Wilde. Ye
gods!
Queen Victoria drinks her tea out of her
saucer ; William of Germany eats sausages held in
his fingers.
According to Anglo-Australians in London,
Sir William Robinson is coming out as a short-
story writer.
A Melbourne schoolmaster has just written
a book stating that Sir William Don was the great
political agitator.
The Prince of Wales did not send a present
to either young Tranby-Croft Wilson or his bride
on their marriage.
Deeming’s canary will bo a legendary bird,
and will cut up into as many as the Waterloo
Heroes, or the Holy Coat.
Booth’s Salvation matrimonial
bureau looks like a dodge to get himself a new
wife.
“God has always been my friend.’’—
B. Deeming. “ God is my ally.” —Kaiser
Wilhelm. Thus do the epileptics rave.
“Williams” is remarkable for wrinkles
round the eyes. He might be 00. judging from
his profile; 40, full face and in a bad light.
Byron’s “lauthe,” Lady Charlotte Bacon,
who died in South Australia, was an aunt of actor
Cates-who, by the way, looks very like a shirt-
collar poet himself.
Dean Macartney, Melbourne, lias just
t >pped his 03, and yet preaches a vigorous sermon.
Contrast his manly tones, in the Cathedral, with
the “ Darly Belawved Bwethwen.”
The Empress Frederick scut an exqusite
“ floral tribute ” for Morell Mackenzie’s funeral.
But then he had secured for her an imperial
diadem and £40,000 a year for life.
There havebeen27 lunatics in the Bavarian
royal family during the last hundred years. That
is only reckoning these of the direct line ; colla-
teral lunatics and idiots by marriage are not
counted.
“General” Booth lately said that a patent-
medicine once established meant a fortune. Wo
often marvelled why he who knows so well the
power of quackery, hasn’t brought out Booth s
pills and ointment.
Mr. Sam. Bcgg (a Maorilandcr), who
contributed drawings to the first numbers of Th'J
Bulletin', has now obtained considerable promin-
ence in London as a black-and-white artist. His
chief field of operations appears to be in Black
and White.
Abraham Abrahams, the well-known
Jewish identity of Adelaide, died suddenly last
week. He was one of the best judges of a good
picture in all Australasia, and had an excellent
collection at his quaintly-arranged bachelor’s
quarters at the York Hotel.
Mr. Altson, a gold-medalist of Victorian
National Gallery, has had a painting, “Echo,
accepted by the Paris Salon, where it will be hung
on tlie line. Alas ! The really brilliant student
never comes back. Australia is a good place for
butchers, but artists starve.
The vivandiere of the Eureka Stockade
rebellion (Vic.) died at Ballarat on fith inst., in
the person of Mrs. Hayes, who assisted at the
amputation of Peter Lalor’s shattered arm, and
who afterwards threw that limb down an aban-
doned shaft and helped to keep the insurgent
leader in concealment.
George Cass, M.L.A. (N.S. W.), died last
week, comparatively young (48 years). George—
a newspaper man —was a bit of a Bohemian in his
pre-legislative days. He was an excellent player
of the English concertina, and was not ashamed
to earn his living in the open air by the exercise
of that accomplishment.
Senior-Constable Sutton, of Orange,
N.S.W., inspector of local slaughter-houses, has
a sleepless eye on the matter of cancerous
cattle. The policeman who prevents a cancerous
beast from passing into consumption stops more
human suffering, and is of greater service to the
community than he who arrests a burglar or a
murderer.
Bookstall Smith left £1,750,000 personalty.
His entire estate totals close on four millions. His
wife and daughters get £200,000 in all; his only
son (what a catch he will be !) is to have the balance
when he reaches 23 years—four years hence. The
late Smith’s father only left £35,000, so that this
colossal fortune was practically all made by the
man who has just died.
Graham Berry is more bandy, a little bit
crippled on his pins, and there is a trifling reaction
after the youthful bound, d la Disraeli, which ho
made on the Victorian shores a few weeks back.
Baldness displays a knob on the back of his head,
formerly coexistent, or unknown. His belltopper
still has its indescribably jaunty set, sidelong and
backward a little, balanced on the knob. His un-
married daughters have grown up into accom-
plished young ladies, after six years in London,
and the eldest takes an intelligent interest in
politics.

Dairy tuple, M.L.A. for Mackay (Q.), in
the parliamentary debate respecting the undesira-
bility of a Judge adjudicating cases in which his
son was an interested barrister, unearthed a neat
anecdote:—
When Chief Baron O’Grady, of the Irish Court of
Exchequer, and afterwards Viscount Guillamore, was on
the Pencil, his son specialised his father’s Court. A brief
to move a motion of course in the Exchequer was sent to
a Mr. Cooper, but the motion was refused hy the Chief
Baron, whereupon Cooper returned the brief and fee to
the solicitor, asking him to send them to Mr. O’Grady,
who next morning moved the motion, which was imme-
diately granted by his father. “Why, my Lord,” said
Cooper, who was in the Court, “your Lordship refused to
grant this motion when I moved it yesterday morning.”
“But, Mr. Cooper,”- said the Chief Baron, unabashed,
“ you must admit that Mr. O’Grady put the case in a
different light.” “Oh,” said Mr. Cooper, soltu voce, “I
presume in the light of the son.”
The inhabitants of Andrew-street, WindSo r
(Melb.), rendered immortal by Deeming’s deeds,
are so insensible to fame that they want to change
the street’s name, but they are reminded of the un-
lucky attempt to obliterate Pentridge in Coburg,
and one thinks of the celebrated Joshua Bngg, who
changed his name to Norfolk Howard. Likewise
of the Rugeley people who, after Surgeon Palmer
had poisoned a man in their town, went to Lord
Palmerston and asked for leave to alter the name
of Rugeley. “Call it Palmerston,” neatly sug-
gested Pam.
John Dunn, of Mount Barker, S.A., the
other day celebrated his 90th anniversary by
giving the town of Mount Barker a park, and

presenting all the employes of Dunn and Co. who
had achieved five years of service with cheques
representing sums varying according to the periods
of their connection with the firm. In recalling to
memory various reunions of the firm, the old man
reminded the men of the jolly time they had at
one Christmas picnic, when he had a big pudding,
made with two cwt. of flour and other ingredients
in proportion, passed into a boiler through the
manhole. He was not cook enough to know
that the pudding would swell, and he wouldn’t
have been" able to get it out again without first
cutting it up had it not been for the ingenuity of
some runaway sailors who wore on hand. On
another occasion, they had roasted a bullock
whole, and had to utilize a big water-wheel to
turn the roast. John Dunn boasted that his firm
had not spent .£lOO in law-disputes ever since
it started in 1844. The patriarch’s birthday
occurred on Feb. 13th, but the recent death
of his eldest son caused him to postpone tire
ceremony above referred to. At one time, he
intended to leave his men legacies at his death,
but his lawyer advised him that these would make
his will cumbersome. There were 21 managers of
the firm’s branches present; 57 employes of over
nine years’ service, and as many more over five
years.
Montague Scott, the well-known artist
whose work has for the last six or seven years
been largely seen in Brisbane periodicals, has
returned to Sydney.
It is announced by cable that the Czar
intends to take more stringent measures for the
suppression of treasonable practices in Russia.
The measures hitherto taken have consisted in
knouting the offender and then hanging him with
a rope which broke three times, so that he was
killed partly by concussion against the floor and
partly by slow and clumsy strangulation. Prob-
ably the Czar means to provide in future that the
rope shall break six times, and intends to fix a
spike under the gallows for the prisoner to fall
upon.
Tranter's Shot.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“I SHOT him like a dog !” said Tranter as he got
off his horse and proceeded to unsaddle.
“Who did you shoot?” asked the new super,
who was standing by.
“Never mind,” returned Tranter, “I’m not
going to give myself away, but I shot him like a
dog. ’
There was bad blood between Tranter and the
new super., and, as Tranter was about leaving,
he was far from respectful in his manner.
The new super, was a young man from the
South, and Tranter was an old Gulf hand.
The new super, was a black-protector, tem-
perance-advocate, and objected to swearing.
Tranter, of course, to sustain his character as an
old Gulf hand, swore the most blood-curdling
oaths in hi< presence, and told the most awful lies
ho could invent about black atrocities. Conse-
quently, they fell out, and Tranter was leaving
the station.
“Now, look here,” said the super., “I’ll get to
the bottom of this—l’ll just follow your tracks
and find out what you have been up to.”
“You’ll find him, safe enough,” said Tranter,
“ he won’t get away.”
The wrathful superintendent had his horse
brought up, and started back on Tranter’s track,
taking another man with him. The trail was not
hard to follow, as Tranter had been after horses
and they had come in along a cattle-track.
They had gone about five miles when a loud,
wailing cry suddenly startled them. They were
in scrubby country at the foot of a low, con-
glomerate rise, with many boulders about.
Following the cry they came to an old gin
seated on the ground cutting herself, or endeavour-
ing to do so, with a piece of broken glass and
occasionally uttering the wail that had first
attracted their notice. Green, the super., knew
that this was a sign of mourning, and guessed
that he was on the right track.
“There’s been murder here,” he said, dismount-
ing and approaching the gin. She took no notice
of him, but kept on moaning and scraping at her
breast.
“ Let’s look about,” said the man, “ they always
go on like this, and we can’t stop her.”
They searched awhile without result, the gin
still keeping up her lamentation. Then Green,
having made up his mind that a vile outrage had
been committed, remounted, and they cantered
back to the station.
“ I will get F ,” he said, naming the native-
police-inspector, “ to bring a trooper and search.”
The “barracks” were, only some three miles
from the station, and F was soon up there
with his smartest tracker.
Meantime, Green had been trying to get out of
Tranter what he had done with the body of his
victim,
“ I shot him like a dog, and I buried him like a
dog was all he could get in answer. “Go and
find him, he won’t run away !”
Green was infuriated, but he knew he could do
nothing until evidence of the murder was estab-
lished.
With one delay and another it was late in the
afternoon ere Green, F , and the black trooper
arrived at the scene of the tragedy. The old gin
was still sitting there raising her requiem song,
but the black boy could obtain no information
from her.
“Some fellow bin go bung,” was all he was
assured of.
They searched without avail until dusk, and
then had to depart unsatisfied, the most astonish-
ing thing being that they could find no other
black’s tracks but those of the gin. Green took
counsel with F , but he could say nothing,
except that the factof what was possibly onlyempty
blow on Tranter’s part, and the presence of the
gin raising the death-wail, was not sufficient
evidence to obtain a warrant on. For himself he
thought from what he saw that Tranter had shot
a blackfellow there, but his belief would go for
nothing. However, he slept at the station and
promised to renew the search as early as
Green passed a sleepless night. ' Hero* 81 >e ’
chance right into his hands of vindicaC as , a
opinions as to the murderous treatment
natives, and beseemed most unaccountably L ~ t!| e
He vowed that he would leave no stone mo t(l-

BINGHI.
A Letter from the Elder Expedition
Editor Bulletin, —l was painfully surprised if
what I consider your uncalled-for sneers at tl .
Elder exploring party, and I feel it is my dutvM!
a simple act of justice, to make public the follow
mg letter, received by me some weeks since from'
one of that gallant, though pe haps foolhardy
party. I trust no true-born Australian will re .;,i
this dark chapter of hairbreadth and marvellous
escapes, in a wild and trackless wilderness win,
out a great pride in the sagacity and endurance of
his countrymen. My knowledge of the writer
gives me good ground for assuring you that it is
improbable the picture is in any respect over-
drawn. I only extract from my friend’s letter
such parts as will interest students of geography
and lovers of the marvellous in Nature.-—Yours
Boiling Biliy’
“ Dear Billy,—l don’t day of the month 'the
month or even the year for that matter and as to where
1 am can only stale that we are camped on a small river
which we just discovered last night. Our bo;s savs it
will make a great alteration in the map of Australia ' \V e
crossed the bridge and went 2 miles up along the West
bank and camped here near where the telegraph line
crosses. Been very busy lately, discovered all last month
all this month, and intend to discover for some time to
come. We have discovered a darkskinned hlaclifellow who
seems toinfest these regions, filling theO’possom with dread
and anxiety, and the Kangaroo with a sense of nuichinsecu-
rily. Curious blacks these, their faces would appear to have
been suddenly confronted by a brick-wall sometime when
they were in a hurry. This will also account for the fact,
that they don’t hurry anymore. They have a queer slick
like a rainbow which the male black—there is a female
black—will throw out to the West in the morning and it
will come back from the East at dinnertime with 2
O possums hanging over it, or the hind-quarters of a
kangaroo, or in war-time it may return with another
blackfellow ; these thing, of course, depending on the
w ill of the operator. 1 have seen this weapon follow a
native-cat 3 times round a tree and when the Cat retired
in a hole it went back for a tomahawk. Our boss is no
look Whether these blacks are cannibals or not lam
unable to say. There was almost a panic in the camp the
other day when we heard there was no more condensed
milk, fancy being without condensed milk away out
here in these dense and impenetrable jungles near the
boundary'-fence of Gooleechi station. We were all going
on strike when the boss discovered a box of it under his
hunk. Some weeks ago we discovered a large sand hill
and also some huge gums which bore the appearance of
never having been seen before by white men We also by
lucky accident stumbled across a splendid place for a
siwmill, the only drawback being the fact that there is
no timber w ithin 90 miles of it, but our boss says this can
easily be overcome by planting trees suitable for building
purpos s and irrigating them until ready for use. This
would find work for a large number and thus this
wilderness we have opened up would scon be the
scene of busy villages and happy homes. I was ill
the front ranks when we discovered this place. We have
discovered a terrible amount of ground lately', why you
wouldn’t believe the quantity of ground I here Is in this
country if only properly discovered. Sydney, Adelaide,
and Melbourne can grow a lot yet. I can discover better
every day'. Some time ago we discovered 2 shearers with
packhorses and I tell you they were glad to see us as we
were the only white men they had met for 7 mortal
hours. They had actually existed for 49 days on tea,
sugar, flour, meat, and other things. One half the world
doesn’t know how the other half lives. Well, I must go
down to the bridge and meet Cobh and Co.’s coach. The
driver gave ns some useful information from time to time
as to the whereabouts of waterholes, outstations, hmin-
daryriders’ huts, pubs., blacks' camps, and good feed for
the camels. He also warned ns against leaving open
gates in the boundary fences, a thing about which ex-
plorers cannot be too cautious. He says we have improved
greatly these last few weeks in this respect. Well, no
more at present, will write again next month if alive.
“ Your friend,
“BOB NELSON.”

An impudent circular has travelled round
the Victorian civil servants urging them to “spot,”
as voteis, the candidates who would reduce their
pay. The Cabinet is furious. “We have 30,000

Clever, ready David Gaunson has decided
very late in the day to stand again for his old
constituency—South Melbourne. We hope to see
him elected. David was a thorn in both sides, but
the public benefited by it.

Poor old Jack Gardiner, member for
“ football,” is evidently doomed in favour of
Bromlev, labour-candidate. His meeting greeted
him with howls and yells, and actually laughed at
his time-honoured “I played for Carlton ten
years ago.” Poor Jack is in favour of every thing,
if they will only put him in. But they won’t.
J. G. Drake’s amendment that Griffith’s
Kanaka Bill be read a second time six months
hence, was, of course, lost —by 31 to 16. Allowing
for three vacancies (Musgrave, Bulimba, and the
prospectively vacated Burrum) two absences in
England (Archer and Ferguson), and the Speaker’s
non-vote, nine available members were absent
from a division which countenanced slavery.

—Benalla and Yarrawonga—by a hitherto un-
known local man. The second-vote-for-bricks-
and-mortar politically slow Campbell.
No more Chinese are allowed to enter
the United States. From 1855 to ’67 the yellow
agony dribbled in at from 3000 to 7000 a year;
from ’6B to ’Bl it mounted to from 10,000 to
20,000. In 1882 there were 33,614, but then came
a Restrictive Act, and in 1883 there were only
381, and in 1886, but 17. Yet, in 1888, came the
Act to exclude all Chinese workmen, and even
that isn’t enough.
Premier Griffith’s comfort to Queensland
workers :—“Make up your minds to accept lower
wages; take whatever wages you can get and
work will be found for you.” And then?
The Silver Age (Broken Hill, N.S.W.)
running a Separation movement on its own
account. It advocates the creation of the Barrier
District into an independent province. The way
in which the N.S.W. Government has blown hot
and cold alternately in respect to the Broken Hill
water supply has done more than anything else to
give the lately formless void of the agitation
definite shape and cognisable dimensions.
Anent Moderator White’s late deliverance,
somewhere in his “History of the Girondists”
Lamartine apostrophises : —“The Church, that
sycophant who ever allies herself to Power and
Oppression!” Apart from this, is it not note-
worthy how circumscribed is the average sacerdotal
vision when jolted out of its rut ? Let the “cloth ”
but open its mouth in secular counsel, and we see
how little it concerns itself with the hard, mun-
dane problems of life.
Dear Bulletin, —Why not advocate tho
abolition of fines as an alternative to imprison-
ment? A case occurred in Melbourne the other
day where a man was prosecuted for an aggravated
assault on an old woman. Prior to opening pro-
ceedings he offered .£lO to square the affair. The
bench, however, merely fined him 40s. and he went
his way chuckling. The “fine” alternative is
merely a device for rendering tire rich independent
of the law, which becomes applicable only to poflr
persons.—Yours &c., Chas. Junoe.
At South Melbourne Town Hall last week,
a voter with a wild, beery eye introduced a novelty
in election-meeting humour. He climbed upon
the platform, and solemnly asked for a drink of
wafer from the candidate’s bottle. The flow of
eloquence was interrupted whilst the Mayor came
forward and served this strange apparition with a
quencher, wh reupon he turned to rejoin the
meeting, and fell off the platform with great
dramatic effect. fThe water had upset him.

barristerial career of his son, to whose constant
appearance before him in court strong objection
has -been taken. And in the masterful Chief-

A Maoriland Conservative, named John
Grigg. announces in the papers that he—Grigg—
is offended at sundry statements of a democratic
character made by Governor Onslow in his vale-
dictory speech, and arises to protest against them.
“If I had been present,” says Mr. Qrigg, “I
would have risen and simply said, ‘ What, sir, are
you saying?’ and then I would have left the
room.” And then, we presume, the lights would
have turned blue, and Onslow and all his family
would have fallen down dead, with a resounding
crash, and hairy spectres would have fled across
the table accompanied by wailing ghosts and old,
earth-smelling skeletons, and there would have
been alarms and incursions, and tumultuous
horror would have brooded over the scene
generally.
Little Nuttall, who will stand for West
Richmond (Melb.) on the Progressive League
ticket, is a very fountain of co-operation statistics
and a powerful talker on all labour questions.
Regarded simply as an apostle of the working-
man, Billy Nuttall wants a lot of beating, and
although (judging from his services to the defunct
Equitable Co-operative Society) he would make
the worst premier on record, he is eminently well
qualified to attack each and every Government at
its weak spots. Moreover, Nuttall has the re-
commendation of being a natural Republican,
who would never wish to dine with Lord Hope-
toun, or any other bloated aristocrat, except,
perhaps, for the sake of revenging himself upon
the vittles.
One Macplievson, an alleged Radical can-
didate for Dandenong and Berwick (Vic.) got
upon his large, fiat, abused feet, the other night,
to explain that he was “ in favour of one-man-one-
vote, as all men are equal in God’s sight and the
Queen’s sight.” Asked to explain what the
dickens his Gracious Sovereign had to do with the
equality of man, this democratic groveller replied,
“She gave us the land.” The torpid denizens of
Dandenong were struck by the passing idea that
Macpherson was an ass, and laughed, but they
gave a respectful hearing to the rest of his drivel,
and seemed to think they had got hold of a first-
class politician to save their bleeding country.
Victoria must be in a parlous state when a Mac-
pherson can pass as a valuable specimen of
advanced liberalism in the sight of God and the
Queen.
Kanakilgriff s (Q.) Pacific Island Labourer 8
Extension Bill repeals the old clauses providing
stoppage of Kanaka immigration, and pretends to
Restrict the slave-trade by providing that “ ‘ tropical
r semi-tropical agriculture ’ shall not include any
work in connection with the cultivation of maize,
or the work of ploughing, or any work done
within a sugar-factory or sugar-refinery.” The
.Bill permits Kanakas to be re-engaged immediately
one term of agreement expires, and deals with
paying (in goods or money) wages due to dead
Kanakas to their relatives or friends in the islands.
Better order them to be paid to the Man in the
Moon. The “Polynesian Fund” has been a
standing joke of yore in Queensland—an instru-
ment specially devised for the greedy embezzler
and defaulter.

at Blairgowrie, Scotland, a few weeks hack. They

“ Bob ” Lyons, solicitor, of Rockhampton,
Q., died on Bth inst. He was a prominent Central
Queenslander, was lured into Parliament by the
Billy-Pattison clique in ’BB, and was further
induced by alleged friends to invest at boom
prices in Mount Morgan gold shares, on 12-
months’ bills, which ruined him in .’B9 to the
extent of sending his estate to the hammer, and
causing him to resign his parliamentary seat.
Last year, he was the solicitor employed in the
defence of the unionist shearers who were legally
“persecuted” by the Coalition Government, of
which good-dog Towzer was the particular depart-
mental deputy for the “military operations.”
We have received from the publishers,
Messrs. Gordon and Gotch, the Australian
Handbook for 1892. It is an elegant volume in a
green cover, with an emu and a kangaroo on the
back, and a golden sun rising in the distance. The
interior or bowels of the publication contains a
vast fund of information concerning Australia,
Maoriland, Fiji, and the outlying islands. It in-
cludes a description of every town, from 40 in-
habitants upwards, throughout these provinces,
maps of every place, masses of hard fact about
tariffs, banks, cab-fares, consuls, ambassadors,
Agents-General, federation, Australian securities,
mails, Mahommedans, newspapers, population,
hogs, rainfall, stamp-duties, churches, municipali-
ties, minerals, climate, excise, land-regulations,
shipping, governors, telegraphs, tramways,
revenue, live-stock, natural history, parcels post,
drawbacks, philanthropy, and lots of other sub-
jects. There is nothing in it that can bring a blush
to anybody’s cheek, and the volume can bo put
into the hands of the young without any fear of
moral contamination. The interest is sustained
to the end, and the story winds up happily.
The Possicker.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I often think of straight, old Lanky Dan,
A fossicker whate’er fate might bequeath him ;
A grim and grizzled worshipper of “ pan,”
Who deemed all other industries beneath him.
Bare-boned and hard, with thin, long hair and beard,
With horny hands that gripped like iron pliers ;
A clear, quick eye, a heart that nothing feared,
A soul full simple in Us few desires.
No hot, impatient amateur was Dan,
Sweating to turn the slides up every minute—
He knew beforehand how his stuff would pan.
Could tell by instinct almost what was in it.
I’ve known him stand for hours and rock and rock,
Swinging now the shovel, now the ladle,
So sphinx-like that at time he seemed to mock,
Resolved to run creation through his cradle.
And when the sun burned fiercely, like a flame,
Or when the rain ran dancing on the river,
He churned the wash or cradled on the same
For cold and heat alike Dan thanked the Giver.
No sun-shafts pricked him through his seasoned hide,
Nor cold nor damp could bend his form heroic ;
Bare-breasted Dan the elements defied,
And met all fortunes like a hoary Stoic.
Where there were tailings, tips, and mangled fields,
And sluggish, sloven creeks meandering slowly ;
Where puddlers old and sluice-sites promised yields,
There Lanky might be found, contented wholly.
E'en tho’ they’d worked the field, as Chinese do,
Had “ bulled” each shaft and scraped out every gutter,
Burnt every stick and put the ashes through,
Yet Dan contrived to knock out bread and butter,
And something for a dead-broke mate—such men
As Dan have little love for filthy lucre ;
His luxury was a whisky now and then.
And now and then a friendly game of euchre.
They tell me he is dead: “On top? That’s so,
Died at the handle, mate, which is accordin’
As he should die, an’ if you’re good you’ll know
Dan’s pannin’ prospects in the River Jordan.”
E. D.
Robert Barber appeared before Judge
Bundey (S.A.) last week, and got seven years for
stealing a dressing-case. Barber annexed the
article from an open window, pawned it for 45.,
and went to Adelaide police-station and gave
himself up.
Cabled that the U.S. Consular Court at
Yokohama (Japan) lias justified the slaying by
Naval-Lieut. Hetherington of an English banker
who seduced that officer’s wife. When “women’s
rights ” are formulated into a practical code, this
standard of justifiable homicide would necessarily
include the killing of one woman by another for
seducing the latter’s husband, wouldn’t it? Again,
if a woman bo held guiltless of the violent death
of a man who attempts her honour, in what legal
estimation should a man be regarded who killed a
wicked woman with designs upon him? In all
these matters, as in the ease of the coming accouche-
ment in prison of Mrs. Osborne, the pearl-thief,
Man, according to his present lights, does not and
cannot consider the sexes as being upon the same
plane of responsible volition.
What People Say.
MRS. MORTON SMITH, MARY-
BOROUGH, QUEENSLAND.
Writes ; “ Having for many years lived a life of
misery, and never during that time to have known
a moments freedom from pain, I am so grateful
for the benefit I have derived from Clements
Tonic, that I write the following particulars of
my case : About four years ago I was attacked
with facial neuralgia, so acute that I was unable
to sleep for 17 days. 1 lost flesh very rapidly and
was reduced to a shadow, and my family thought
tiie end was near, so weak, pale and miserable
had I become ; I could see their anxious looks and
hear them sobbing when away from me, but before
my face they were all courage and smiles, so
thoughtful were they of my feelings. I had three
doctors, but they did me no good; the misery
still continued. At last a friend recommended
Clements Tonic so strongly that I procured a
bottle; and after the third dose the pain was
relieved. I continued its use and took a good
many bottles and gradually recovered my wasted
strength, and am now as well as ever I was in my
life. lam sure Clements Tonic is the sole cause
of my being alive, for even the doctors told my
family that it would be better to settle my earthly
matters as the end was near; but thanks to
Clements Tonic they were wrong again.
“Mrs, Morton Smith.”
We thank Mrs. Smith for allowing us to publish
her testimony. Fancy the misery and torture of
■sleeplessness for a period of seventeen days, wake-
fulness being enforced by the agony of neuralgia,
and all the time an infallible remedy within reach.
We trust her letter will bring the same relief to
other sufferers.

Cl CASTLEREAGH STREET, SYDNEY.
Jas, A. Walsh, Proprietor
(Lata of the Occidental Hotel, San Francisco}.
Jas. A. Walsh and his assistants, American barkeepers,
continue to cater to the Sydney Public, and their &ni
Fizzes, Champagne Cocktails, Milk Punches, Manhattan
Cocktails, are the rage of the town. Mr. Walsh, while
thanking his friends for their generous patronage, begs
to inform them that only the best brands of Spirits,
Wines, and Cigars are sold at this house. If you have
not tried one of Walsh’s Gin. Fizzes this hot weather
vou had better do so.
Sociery
Why didn't the daily papers give the
names of the “ladies” who, for sheer plea-
sure, attended the big Melbourne lust-and-
murder show —the sweet creatures who laughed
so loudly when the witness deposed that he
told “Williams” that he would hang “as
sure as God made little apples ” —and, who
screamed so shrilly from their sense of “ outraged
decency ” when the miserable prisoner told the
coroner to put the verdict in his pipe and smoke
it? It would be well to have a catalogue of the
harpies present on pleasure bent, because when
Woman Suffrage has come and women get their
“rightswe shall know where to procure a hang-
woman or two.

Frederick Bayley Deeming knows in his
hearth of hearths whether he is guilty or not,
* * *
The “lady journalists” came out very
strong at the Windsor murder inquest, and sat
round the prisoner with open note-books as big as
ledgers. Each brainy lady had a fixed glare in
her eye, which seemed to defy the heavy villaifi of
the piece, and say : “You wouldn’t do it to me,
sir ! ” Williams’ optical reply said, “ I wouldn’t
like to.”
* * m
F. B, Deeming : “ My dear little wife is
not dead at all. She has only gone for a trip, and
will return by-and-bye to heap confusion on all
you liars.” And what about “ loving Katie ?”
* # #
Marshall Lyle has seized on the published
fact thatj Deeming was known as “Mad Fred.”
in his youth, and is working up an “insanity ”
defence. Already he has cabled to England for
the early life of his client, and means to ask for an
adjournment on 22nd inst., when the case comes
on in the Criminal Court.
* * *
The Wollongong (N.S.W.) Argus can
spare time to devote a leader to the price of milk,
while the Williams-Deeming horror still spirts
gouts of gore; of which, not a stray syllable in the
issue. Thank Heaven, there is one Australian
country editor whose mind remains childlike dur-
ing the Boom of Blood.
» * «

missioner Chomley intend to take with regard to
the “ revelations ” of that reliable officer, Detec-
tive Brandt concerning Deeming’s South African
career ?
# # *
Deeming’s detectives drove him from St.
Kilda pier to Melbourne via Andrew - street,
Windsor, the scene of the murder, but Frederick’s
face, though carefully watched, gave no sign.
Which reminds one that when Cunningham, the
cold-blooded murderer of “Frank the Austrian,”
was being escorted from Port Douglas (Q.), where
he was arrested, back to Thorn borough, on the
Hodgkinson, near where the crime was com-
mitted, the party camped for a night at the very
tree under which Mr. Cunningham slew his man.
And Police-Inspector Icely carefully chained up
Mr. Cunningham all night to that tree.

• * #
Deeming’s library, so far as can be ascer-
tained, consisted of a “History of the Inquisition,”
the “ Criminal Calendar,” and the Sydney Morn-
ing Herald, which hitherto stately journal has
disgusted its daily contemporaries by the en-
thusiasm with which it has revelled in the “ sensa-
tionalism ” which (in other people) it is ever wont
to denounce. By the way, there was a project
afloat in Sydney the other day for the establish-
ment of a Police News, but the scheme didn’t
come off. No wonder. Now that the Herald is
fairly “ on the job ” there is no room for anything
else in the blood-and-thunder line.
By the way, the London Times, during the
progress of Dr. Neild’s peep-show, had 2000
words cabled from Melbourne daily. And it
howled for more.
« * *
The newspaper-man whom Williams loudly
asked, at the Coroner’s court, for “that five
shillings he owed him over the last game of nap”
must have blushed considerably. By the way, on
a beam in the cabin occupied by Williams on the
R.M.S. Ballarat is still to be seen the accused’s
pencil record of his Nap account. The ship
people are not painting this over but are taking it
to England as a curiosity.

Hero is a charming hunk of medical evi-
dence which turned up at the Windsor murder
inquest: “ Dr. Mullen deposed that as to the axe
he would not like to say it produced the cut on
the back of the head. Nor would he like to say
that this particular axe could have produced a
depressed fracture of the skull. It could certainly
produce a very serious fracture of the skull, for he
had tried it on a dead body. 1 ’ Charming occupa-
tion, going round putting heads on dead men with
an axe just to see how the blessed thing would
work, dontcherknow»
• • #
Nothing and nobody, except himself, can
prevent Deeming’s committing suicide, however
strict and unceasing the surveillance. It is a
truism that true love takes no cognisance of the
merit or demerit, the humanity or inhumanity,
the morality or criminality, of its object; and
that it is strengthened by the trouble or danger of
the beloved. Deeming requested an interview with
his quondam sweetheart, saying that upon her
answers to certain questions that he would ask
her at this interview depended his subsequent
happiness. She proved the amount and the
constancy of her affection by refusing to grant the
interview. The coldness of her reply is quite
sufficient to break the heart of a man with Deem-
ing’s egregious vanity. He can, at any time he
likes, let the lady’s cruelty begin to operate, and
become subject to a violent outburst of grief, and
let his heart give two or three tremendous thumps
against his ribs, and split, and thus spill his blood
all over his lungs, and saturate his thorax. In
short, he can commit suicide by sentiment.
• * *
In re Deeming’s alleged secret marriage
in Sydney to a girl under 21 years, whom he abso-
lutely deserted within a month, the clergyman
who officiated at the marriage and tells the story

Will Miss Kounsevell now go back
to her first love, the Brisbane young man ?
We don’t know who he is, but we can see him

The young lady aforesaid escaped several things
besides being cemented in the bonds of holy matri-

man just returned from that region. He says it
is an awful, barren place, which wouldn’t grow
even a thistle, and where he had to walk 30 miles
to water his horse.
• • »
Up to date, every witness in the Deeming
case has identified everything put before him,
barring one man, who didn’t seem to be dead
certain about the canary. When the average
witness gets fairly started on an exciting case, he
goes the whole hog. If you bring up forty men
to swear to a first-class murderer, and each one of
them only got a passing glimpse of him in the
dusk some three years before, thirty-nine of them
will testify to him right off, and the other one
will only be troubled with a very slight hesita-
tion. When they once get started they will
identify the old boots he had on in 1876,
and the shirt he sent to be washed at the time
of the Crimean War, and a brick out of the house
he lived in when a boy, and never once turn a
hair. Some of them, if they got sufficiently
excited, would identify his breath in a bottle.
The identification business goes altogether too
easily. If one or two witnesses would be just a
shade less positive now and then the public would
have a lot more faith in it; as it is, there is a
lurking suspicion abroad that when once a man is
put in the dock, not only everybody who saw him,
but also everybody who didn’t see him, is ready
to rush forward and swear for the mere thrill of
the tiling. /
I • # *
Melbourne Age, Telegraph, Herald, and
Standard, and Sydney Evening News (the last-
named under an effective nom de mer) were “per-
mitted ” by the “ authorities ” to get up at 5 a.m.
on the Ist of April and board the Lady Loch at
Williamstown. They were also permitted to
labour under the delusion that Mr. Fred. Deeming
was to be received on board from the Ballarat and
landed either at Mordialloc or Bellarine Gardens
in the dead of the night, and they had provided
themselves with overcoats, cough-drops, and mos-
quito-nets accordingly. And, furthermore, no
obstacle was placed in the way of their paying
heavily for breakfast on the Government steamer,
said Government being evidently in earnest in its
policy of retrenchment.
The Sydney D.T. reports the discovery of
a small “ battlcaxe ” among Deeming’s effects.
“Battleaxe” is good, bat we badly want to know
why that bloodthirsty paper didn’t also discover a
stinkpot and a harpoon, a few cross-bows, a suit
of chain mail, a war-horse, a torpedo, some sub-
terranean mines, a war-whoop tied up in brown
paper, sundry entrenchments in a band-box, and
an old Roman galley. These things would have
lent an air of completeness to an otherwise bald
and unconvincing narrative.
* # #
The Earl of Meath, who arrived in the
same steamer as Deemiug, with a missus and a
mission to (Heaven help us !) socially reform Aus-
tralia, is a slender, long-bearded and very bald
brunette, who reads his “addresses” from large
square sheets of MS., which occasionally go
astray and give the “ peroration ” spasms. Meath’s
voice is clear and sing-songy, his matter flowery,
and his ideas dear old friends.

The number of, as yet, well-dressed men
who atop the passer-by and shame-facedly ask fo
“a little bit of tobacco,’’ is increasing in Melbourn
every week. Tobacco dulls the pangs of hunger
for a time.
* * #
Miss Margaret Whyte has beaten all the
male students at Melbourne University in the
finals for medicine and surgery, and now the
Hospital committee is fighting over the admission
of lady resident doctors.
Some of the recent great fraud discoveries
have been made by the merest of flukes, like that
by which the career of the Penshurst (Vic.) big
bank-defaulter was out short some years ago. The
books had been so well “cooked ” that the bank-
inspector had no hesitation in passing them.
However, the manager and the inspector visited
that evening the home of the shire secretary,
where a jolly time was spent. Just ere the party
broke up the conversation turned bn the prosperity
of the district, the secretary instancing that the
shire had £4OOO at its credit. That simple remark
landed the banker in gaol: the inspector remem-
bered noting that the council had only £4 at
credit. So next morning, just as the manager was
about to see him off, and was slapping himself on
the back, the inspector remarked that he “ would
take another look at the books.”
# # •
Birregurra (Vic.) has a curiosity in the
shape of an old gentleman who will be 100 years
old next June, should all go well. He has a large,
well-made coffin ready for himself, which he pre-
pared, however, only some six years ago. He is a
bachelor, lives in a little two-roomed cottage on
some GO acres of freehold, and keeps the coffin on
the rafters of his bedroom —rather a nice prospect
on waking in the morning. The old gentleman is
hale and hearty, and drives into Birregurra every
week by himself, getting in and out of the trap by
the aid of a box which he drops out of, and draws
into, the conveyance by a rope. He is proud of
his old age, and is running for the Victorian
record. Rumour says tiiat when he was making
the coffin he tried it on and got stuck in it, where
he remained for some time, till a neighbour called
and helped him out.

And, by the way, there is talk of one of the
Sydney Sunday papers changing hands. It is a
ghastly thing to be an “ amateur ” newspaper-
proprietor. It costs so much to learn the business.

The Bulletin has received from Messrs.
E. and W. Stewart, jewellers, of Launceston
(Tas.), a circular to the following effect :
We have secured the contract for the supply of the
medals presented to exhibitors at the Tasmanian Inter
national Exhibition. We notice from the list of awards
that The Bulletin has secured a first award which entitles
you to a gold-medal, and as the gold-medals are supplied
at the cost of each exhibitor, we write to ask you to for-
ward cheque or bank draft for the sum of £4 65., price of
medals and cost of postage on same, on receipt of which
we will forward in return per mail a gold-medal. If
desired we can supply gilt-medals to represent gold iu
cases for 12s. each.
We haven’t sent the cheque yet, but wo desire to
ask one or two questions. In the first place we
have several distressed acquaintances who, through
poverty, extreme old age, or gradual decay of the
mental faculties, were unable to exhibit at the
Tasmanian International Exhibition, but who
have a great desire for medals to soothe their last
hours ; also we know a man who is undeservedly
in gaol, and another who sleeps in the Domain, and
who both badly need a medal to help them to bear
up. Therefore we want to know whether, sup-
posing we take ten medals, the firm is prepared to
make any reduction. Also, in the second place, if
we were to take two tons of International Exhibi-
tion medals in order to award one to each of our
leading contributors, what price would the firm be
prepared to accept. Thirdly, we want a quota-
tion for silver medals and certificates of merit-
say 200 of each. Lastly, we want to know -vyhab
price the firm will charge to sell the whole con-
tract, so that we will have the business in our own
hands, and then any time we feel depressed we
can award ourselves a first-class gold medal to keep
our spirits up.

THE CURSE OF DENTIST’S TEETH.
I beg to remind you all here that the best work
public life, in literature, in art, in science is not alwav
done by those who have chewed their meat with dentist •
teeth.’’—Premier Shiels {with his mental eve on v!
Premier Gillies), at Casterton. ex '
All you who try with eye of glass
Your way to steer,
Thro’ city’s seething, surging mass,
Need have no fear.
Your imitation optic, neat,
Conceals beneath
It’s blindness, brains, so don’t you eat
Your butcher’s meat
With dentist’s teeth.
If young or old, we do not care,
Your hat should rest
On beauteous artificial hair.
Of make the best;
Your head may shine in summer hea',
Your wig beneath,
If you’d be wise we ask of you
To promise that you’ll never eat
Yonr morning meal or evening stew,
Your hash, or vvhatso’er you chew,
With dentists’ teeth.
And you who dye your whiskers brown
That should be grey ;
And you who love to come to town
In great display.
Be rogue or fool, be slow or fleet,
We place a wreath
Of flowers of learning, wet with dew,
Upon your head, or at your feet,
But— swear by all that’s black and blue,
Your molars are what nature grew,
Not dentists’ teeth.
J. G.

» # *
Ab a Sydney theatre the other night a
stout lady, during au interval, “jumped ” a man’s
seat, from which she transferred his mackintosh
to her own lap. On his return she offered him
the mackintosh, without the seat. He would have
both or neither; and, if she refused to do as ho
asked, he would prosecute her for theft, having
found her in possession of his property without
his permission. “ But I don’t want the mack-
intosh,” she shrieked. “Of course not, now
you’re found out!” said he; “but 1 find ray pro-
perty shifted from where I left it, and you’ve got
it, which is enough to enable me to call a police-
man and give you in charge, which I shall do at
once if you don’t place the mackintosh where you
found it!” She relinquished the seat.

• * #
Lately, in a N.S.W. Asylum, a slowly-
dying and terribly-tortured patient asked the
doctor for poison. “Doctor, be merciful!” The
doctor put a white powder under the patient’s
I illow. “Take that, and in two minutes you’ll
be in Heaven.” An hour after, the patient was
cold and stiff. Ho had taken what he believed to
be poison and had passed away. Some savage
tribes seem to possess the power of dying at will.
It is the white man who trembles on the brink of
eternal sleep.

A Sydney music-seller advertises in the
daily papers that he wishes to return his best
thanks to a lady with a little boy, who called at
his shop, and overhauled and utterly disarranged
.£5O worth of music so that it had all to be sorted
over again by the assistant, and having finally
bought one piece at threepence, paid for it with a
bad shilling, and got away with ninepeuco change.
This is the most surprising instance of gratitude
we know of.
# • *
James Swan, of Brisbane, left £20,000 to
the Queensland Baptist Association subject to his
wife s life interest in same. A local law requires
the attestation by three witnesses of all such
bequests, also their registration within a month
of testator's death. Neither condition having
been complied with, the Association finds itself
badly “left” and completely dependent on the
pleasure of Mrs. Swan. How civil the clergy will
be to that lady !

Says Gi R. Sims, apropos to the maudlin
drivel inspired by the case of Mrs. Osborne, the
culchawed necklace-stealer :—“ It must be remem-
bered that for the crimes poor women commit
they have a dozen excuses, whereas a lady has
none. If the history of some of the poor, degraded
wretches who figure at the police-courts could be
written up by a sentimentalist in the Daily Tele-
graph, there would be thousands of hearts which
would feel pity for them.- But these poor crea-
tures don’t get any sympathy—they are not
interesting, and they are poor.” Yes, even their
crimes are commonplace. They prig strings of
onions, instead of strings of pearls.
An English society paper reports, among
the confessions of a fashionable lady, that she had
attended over 800 dances in search of a hus-
band, and all she found was fourteen attacks of
"bronchitis, three of pleurisy, and about a hundred
colds in the head. After all this frantic exercise
on the light fantastic toe, she remains a spinster
to this day. Also, she has lost tier complexion
through exposing her spine to innumerable
draughts, and her nose has become red through
tight-lacing, and some of her internal organs have
been entirely danced out of place, and she is an
old rack of bones and subject to chronic rheu-
matism. The woman who goes hunting for a
husband now-a-days in England lias about as
bright a prospect as- if she were running after a
bunyip with a dark lantern.

Says S.M. Herald
It is not so certain after all that it is absolutely neces-
sary to put persons accused of not very desperate crimes
into a criminals’dock, and thus assume them to be any-
thing but innocent before they are proved to be guilty.
But when shall we have a Judge with enough
sense of justice and enough nerve to break
through the rule which compels an accused man
to go behind the spikes? It is open to any one of
Australia’s Judges to make a commencement in
this direction.

That vulgar institution the “Surprise
Party ” appears to be again to the fore. It is
defended by its apologists on the score that it is
“ a joke.” This Bulletin suggests that the next
victim greet his surprise-party with a fire-hose or
a volley of sulphuretted hydrogen. That also
would be a most excellent joke.

* * *
The ferry-service in Sydney harbour is so
congested that a hideous accident through collision
is merely a matter of time. Twenty times a day,
four or five boats leave Sydney Cove at the same
minute; and the breaking of the rudder-chains of
one of these at a critical moment might occasion a
hecatomb. The skippers of the steamers would
tell the Marine Board this, if they were diploma-
tically whispered to while their employers were
away.

# # *
Most Australian Judges accept the evi-
dence of bank-officials as “experts” in hand-
writing. The late Judge Gillies, of Maonland,
had a very poor opinion of the bank-clerk as an
authority on this subject, and invariably, in cases
before him, lithographic artists were called to give
their opinion concerning signatures. Judge
Gillies was probably right. Pew forgers can
“ dasli off ” a signature—in nine cases out of ten
a forged signature is laboriously copied just in the
same way as a lithographer copies signatures is
the exercise of his calling. A lithographer aided
by a magnifying-glass can ahvays tell a copied
signature. The conclusion is obvious.

Graham Berry has said something definite
at last. He would gi ve a vote to every woman
who works for her living.
One of Melbourne’s delusions is that
parents contribute to the support of unruly
children committed to the industrial schools. The
magistrates bounce, and dodging propagators and
trixes cringe, but know it’s all humbug, for the
Government has written off about <£20,000 of debt
on this score. Meanwhile, Paterfamilias reads the
bounce in his morning paper, and says, “Ha!
Quite right! Our magistrates think of the poor
taxpayer.”
# # *
It is a surprising circumstance that among
all the auditors who have been arrested of late for
certifying to false balance-sheets issued by bogus
banks, not one has put in a plea of insanity, com-
plicated by epileptio fits. It is about the most
reasonable plea that such an auditor could put in,
and we hope that the next one will take the hint.
Homicidal mania is already a recognised com-
plaint, but arithmetical mania, with a diseased
tendency on the part of the patient to state that
the securities are all right when they aren’t, is a
matter that hasn’t been fully investigated. The
mentally-distorted w'retch, who inherits his ten-
dency towards auditing from a long line of com-
mercially misshapen ancestors, ought to be an
interesting study.
* * *
The Salvation Army is no longer the
humble, patient, forbearing institution it used to
be; now that it is growing rich it puts On airs.
Its latest development is at Eidsvold (Q.), where
a “soldier” had to be hauled off a little boy,
whom he was attempting to smash because lie
“laughed during the service.” The local paper
remarks that the same meek and rowdy Christian
was recently fined at the police court for assaulting
a cow with an axe. Probably the cow laughed
too.

The jury found prisoner guilty, and Judge Hodges sen-
tenced Hunziker to 12 months’ imprisonment in Geelong

sentence:—
M‘Crovv, convicted at Sydney last week of
the murder of his wife, is probably mad; lie comes of
a mad family. He should not be hanged, unless
the Government are prepared to poll-axe all
lunatics; Hr. Milford, whose word is unquestion-

In the Fortnightly Review, Professor
Lombroso writes about the physical insensibility
of Women* On the question of sensibility to pain
he is clear that woman is inferior. He quotes
Carle to the effect that the majority of women
allow themselves to be operated on with astonish-
ing insensibility, almost as though the body under
the surgeon’s knife were that of another and not
their own; and Dr. Mela to the effect that men
swoon in the dentist’s hands oftener than women.
The average of woman’s life is decidedly longer
than man’s; but the outward expression of moral,
if not physical pain, is more vehement in woman
than in the majority of men.
Trust the housewife for knowing what
suits her best, and when she tells you that a
“New Home ” sewing-machine is equal to a
maid-of-all-work, a written promise of marriage
and a watchdog combined, go straight away and
interview J. W. Johnston & Co., sole agents for
Australia, whose machines have had a reputation
—for 15 years at Haymarket, Sydney, 4 years in
Brisbane, and 3 years in Melbourne, in which
city, by the way, a retail establishment has been
opened to fulfil the orders crowding in. The
“New Home” machines are manufactured by
thousands in Massachusetts, U.S.A., and the
Sewing Machine News (New York) recently
noted that an order for them had been received
from Paris (France) for 15,000 in one consignment,
to be delivered within 00 days. The “ New
Home ” can do every kind of work required by
the most accomplished needle epicure, and is
fitted (according to price) with various adjuncts,
even to a “ button-holing attachment.” Its price
ranges from about =£8 to <£18.
A tribute-party was working some par-
ticularly unlovely country in a Ballarat Fast
mine. The four o’clock shift was unanimously
K.G. (Cousin Jack), and a red-headed evangelist
had been stirring-up the Wesleyaus of the town
and district, and bringing them to a sense of their
unworthiness and a knowledge of the world to
come, consequently the Roley’s shift was suffering
a spasm of piety, and always prayed on the plat
before going in to the face, a circumstance, how-
ever, which did not debar them from the use of
any loose profanity that occasion seemed to
demand. It was almost necessary to keep her
timbered close up, and there was occasion
for a set when the afternoon-shift went on, but
the three felt fortified with righteousness, and
determined to let her go till after crib. They had
not been at work long when an ominous cracking
warned them off. “ Don’t ee be thinking, Brother
Veal, we’d best engage in prayer you?” The
three holy men adjourned unanimously to a safe
place, and Brother Veal indulged in prayer.
Whilst they were at their devotions a lump abcut
the size of an elephant came away from the hang-
ing-wall. Brother Veal stopped short in the midst
of his pious “rassle,” and, looking at his com-
panions, said impressively : “ If any man say
tome, after this, there he no ’fHeavy in prayer
you, I’m goin’ to tell him he’s a liar.” And
ho meant it.
To a woman who works for her living,
Says Berry, the knight,
A vote he would gladly be giving:
’Xvvere certainly right.
He would give her just one
For the work she has done—
He is very polite.
If a fortune she ended by making,
Ritolderolloo !
For a sou who his pleasure was taking
(As gentlemen do).
Should we give to that drone,
Having lands of his own,
A dozen, or two ?
His Honor is a barn6d man
Who sees the force of thrashing:,
And thinks his mighty engine can,
With one judicious lashing,
Regenerate a dreadful beast,
And make the lesson lasting.
Thus thinks the worthy judge—at least,
He thinks so when he’s fasting.
Rut after two helps off the roast,
And just enough of sherry.
Those sentiments give up the ghost-
His Honor’s feeling merry.
He reconsiders this, thinks that’s
Quite too rough on the sinner.
iVho says : “ There are no hungry cats
When man has had his dinner?”
The cat’s unfleshed ; now shall we budge.
And off the truth be casting,
Or make it lawless in a judge
To sentence when he’s fasting?
Full true’s the sentiment we sing—
Deny it if you can, sir—
A dinner is a noble thing
To put heart in a man, sir.
A lady-killer : “ Williams.”

ÜBLICN Q T I C E.
B E D D I N G.
Thero is perhaps no part of House-Furnishing
as to the genuineness and value of which the public
are so entirely dependent upon the practical
knowledge and
COMMERCIAL MORALITY
of vendors as
33ED33IKT&,
and certainly the importance and the necessity for
pure, suitable, and properly manipulated materials
cannot be overrated.
A ricketty and badly finished article of Furni-
ture may annoy, and an ill-designed sideboard or
cabinet offend pood taste ; but, even then, no
serious harm occurs. But with improperly purified
or badly made BEDDING, how different the tale,
for then you have that which is INJURIOUS
to HEALTH, and undoubtedly SPREADS
DISEASE.
See report on Typhoid Fever and other diseases
in Sydney and suburbs from 1876 to 1891.
See Census and Industrial Returns for 1891.
COMPARATIVELY FEW FURNITURE
WAREHOUSES IN SYDNEY
manufacture the bedding they sell, but, buying
from wholesale makers, have to offer their cus-
tomers Made-up Goods, of the contents of which
they actually know nothing.
Let us consider for a moment that one-third of
one s life is spent in bed. Does it not concern you
to know that so great a part of life’s short span
ought to bo carefully and properly provided for?
Have you ever taken the trouble to examine
your mattress to find out what is in it ? Whether
HORSEHAIR or HOG HAIR,
WHITE JAVA KAPOK,
OR ONLY THAT
VERIEST OF RUBBISH,
THE OLD BLACK RAG FLOCK,
Or that short, seedy Indian Kapok called
Cotton Fly ?
Be wise, be wise. Do not let another day pass
until you have thoroughly examined your bedding.
Those Drapers, Ironmongers, Co-operative
Societies, and others who simply take orders for
bedding from the
PRICE LIST OF CHEAP MAKERS
are still worse off, having no knowledge whatever
of its manufacture, and are governed simply by
CHEAP PRICE LISTS,
and in many instances just place the order with
whatever wholesale maker offers them the greatest
discount.
Nor is this the only disadvantage their customers
suffer, as too frequently the bedding is supplied
by small makers, who have neither adequate
machinery to prepare the materials nor suitable
premises in which to carry on the work. Indeed,
if the purchasers of bedding could only see the
condition and surroundings of some of the makers
of bedding they would not allow the bedding to
come within coo-ee of their backyards, let alone
their bedrooms.

hill, have for many years manufactured all Bedding
upon their OWN PREMISES, importing the
materials DIRECT, employing the most improved

GUARANTEE UNPRECEDENTED

THE GREATHEŚ OF LITTLE THINGS
SMALL beginnings often lead to big results
A seemingly unimportant flaw in the founda-
tion of a building, if not discovered and promptly
attended to, may result in the loss of the whole
structure. A little bit of flirtation and a letter or
two full of ‘sweet nothings,” when ingeniously
focussed by a smart lawyer, will often constitute
the foundation on which to base an interesting
breach of promise case. A small but energetic
hen, turned loose in a flower garden, will, in the
course of one brief afternoon, reduce to a condition
of the most hopeless confusion all the artistic
taste resulting from weeks of patient toil on tin-
part of the enthusiastic horticulturist. A little
harmless-looki ng rusty tack in the carpet, with
the business end pointing sky wards, is a trifling
thing, but it plays an important part when taken
in conjunction witli the sole of a barefoot attached
to a man weighing 180 pounds or thereabouts
who has suddenly discovered its location. A
little pain or ttcho is often made light of at first
but by-and-bye, when it has develop! d into a
deep-seated disease, the folly of not regarding its
presence at an earlier stage becomes only too
apparent. A little forethought is a valuable
quality, and the man or woman possessed of it
will always be found forearmed and prepared to
grapple with any emergency, no matter how
unexpected. Hundreds and thousands of useful
lives that would otherwise have been blighted and
rendered not only valueless but physically un-
bearable, by the ravages of such dread complaints
as rheumatism, sciatica, and gout, or through the
improper treatment of those ordinary accidents
that may a ll y day befall, have been preserved and
lengthened by the timely employment of St.
Jacobs Oil, a medical combination of natural pro"
ducts, the specific virtues of which arc nothing
short of marvellous. No need to pay a visit to
the doctor (o have the sprained foot, the aching
back, the neuralgia tortured face, or the accidental
burn or bruise attended to when anyone can with
the aid of this wonderful embrocation successfully.
i 1 h uself in his own home l . The popularity
of fct. Jacobs Oil is evidenced by the enormous
sale it enjoys in every habitable part of the globe,
as well as by the general approbation of those who
have tested and proved its subtle healing proper-
ties, _ including the testimony of clergymen
physicians, judges, athletes, squatters, bushmen!
and toilers in every walk of life.
THE EASTER ENCAMPMENT.
Onr citizen soldiers are clearly determined not
to accept without a final struggle the decision of
the Government to deprive them of their annual
■ shivoo ” under canvas. Members have been
approached on the subject, and the political wires
are being pulled in the hope that a hard-hearted
Ministry may repent even to the elev< nth hour
and not mercilessly crush the fondest ambition of
Australia’s brave defenders. What a bitter dis-
appointment for the fair sex, too, will be this
latest phase of Governmental cheese-paring!
Fancy the anguish of Sophia at not being per-
mitted to see her noble George in full warpaint
on the tented field. The dear girls at any rate,
must be compensated for their share of the loss
in some way. A good plan would be for their
respective husbands, sweethearts, or brothers to
present each with one of our new Sterling Silver or
Rolled Gold \V aterbury Watches. The solatium
would be a pleasing as well as an eminently useful
one, while the cost to the donor need not cause
any anxiety. Ihese watches are little gems, are
beautifully finished, embrace all the very latest
improvements, and are retailed at prices ranging
from 40s. The new gentlemen's watches (series
“ K ”) are companion watches to the above, and
incomparable as accurate timekeepers.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Some ex-Australian cricketers, now in England,
are forming a scratch eleven to tour the country in the
co ning summer.
# * *
Nat Gould’s book “ Hard Hit,” for which the
author has got his own price, will be published at once,
but the title will have to be changed in name, as “Hard
Hit” has already been registered in London.
* * #
Brisbane is favourite for the interprovincial
eight-oar race ; it rows the Cambridge stroke. A son of Sir
Joshua Peter Bell finished at Cambridge and rowed twice
in the eight. He coached the Breakfast Creek crew, and
revolutionised the Queensland system of rowing.
# # *
Rudolph, who in many people’s opinion could
have won Dreadnought’s Derby in ’B9, competed unsuc-
cessfully in a s.'lling race at Aspendale Park (Mel.)last
week. Bather a come-down for a Derby horse. Strange
that none of White’s cast-offs ever win anything for their
buyers, as witness Morok, Titan, Dreadnaught, Rudolph,
Prelude, &c. Another of White’s Derby animals, Volcano,
who started one of the hottest favourites on record for a
Caulfield Cup, and put up the Australian record for six
furlongs (1.14), has also been running unsuccessfully in
selling-races around Sydney. Both of these are geldings.
• * #
Mr. Charles Ueloitte, murdered with George
Clarke by blacks at Creswell Downs (Q.) on 30th Jan. last,
was once well known in Sydney as an amateur oarsman.
# * *
Daring the year the Carlton (Melbourne) foot-
ball club received £1250, though a strictly amateur
team (of course), and can only bring forward a balance of
£ll to the ensuing year. Incidental expenses are only
£709 ; doctor’s fees, £BO.
# • •
Rostellan, at 15 to 1, won the Hawkesbury
Autumn Handicap, run at Rosehill on Saturday. The
mud made it any horse’s day out.
« • •
Peter Boland is dead. Had Peter taken care of
himself, he should have taken a place amongst the first
flight of pugilists at his weight. When he died he
weighed forty-six pounds.
* • *
The Bui Bui and Bronzewing, the crack Sydney
sailed their fourth match on Saturday last,
when the B.onzewiiig again won, after an unsatisfactory
drifting match. They will meet again and again, unto
seventy limes seven.
# * *
They say that Rarnage, who has been standing
down a good deal of late, will get a chance to win the
Sydney Cup on Greygown.
A real “ goud tiling ” did not couio off on
Hawkesbury Handicap day. Just before the second race
the “ books'” were betting on the Hawkesbury Handicap,
crying 5 to 4 (lie field, when a big punter rushed in and
took 50 to 40 about Plain Bill. The “ book” told hisclerk
to hook the wager 50 to 40 Tridentine for the Hawkesbury
Handicap, when the punter said he was backing Plain
Bill for the second race. “Bookie” cried the wager off,
but when wagering really commenced on the second race
the “ books ” had smelt a rat and opened 2to 1 on. Thus
do fools in the shape of big punters rush in where angels
in the shape of astute stables fear to tread.
* * *
The winner of the Rowley Mile carried a
penalty therefor for the first time in the history of the
Hawkesbury Handicap. This year’s conditions fdr the
race were so vaguely worded, that the owner of Triden-
tlne carried a penalty in preference to running the chance
of a disqualification.
• • »
To lecture such an artist as Mick Harris seems
“ a hit off,” but the writer, as a backer in a small way of
Tridentine, was anything hut pleased to see the animal,
running in mud fetlock-deep, with Ost. 51' >. in the saddle,
raced to the front, and the result was only as might have
been expected.
# • •
The Oxford -.Cambridge University boat-race
this year makes the 20th win for Oxford against Cam-
bridge’s 22. The Putney to Mortlake record was broken ;
t ime' Unu. 21 sec., or 14 seconds faster than any previous
achievement, The Cambridge crew were beaten by 1J
leivMh«, the victory being against the start betting, a
condition! of prognostication v Inch is very rare indeed iri
connection with ’ Varsity matches. The year ’77 stands as
that of the only dead heat between the college crews, and
that happened because the Oxford “ bow ” caught a crab
and sprung his oar at the very finish, when the win
seemed assured.
* # #
The cable states that Johnel and Corbett have
signed articles for a fight to take place at New Orleans, on
the 7th September, the stakes to be £9OOO. There ae
few men who sign articles more frequently or more cherr-
fully than Johnel, but the fight seems to get] further
away every time he signs.
« » *
Cavill beat M‘lndoe in the two-out-of-three
events swimming-contest at Adelaide. MTndoe won the
100 yards, but Cavill scored easy victories in the two
others.
« # •
Three Sydney bookmakers were recently travel-
ing in a railway carriage. Two were playing nap, the
third was looking on. One deal gave each exceptional
hands. The caller had the four chief hearts and the ace
of clubs; the dealer five small hearts. “I’ll go nap”
said the caller. “Twenty pounds you lose,” said the
onlooker. “ Done !” He led the ace of clubs. The on-
looker fairly kicked himself. By looking at both players’
hands and then laying the wager he had divined to the
expert the cards which the opponent held.
ON PAPER.
Mark the flashes on the distant
Strip of velvet green ;
Itainbow colours 'bine persistent
In the shimmering sheen—
Round the crimson banner muster
Heroes of the turf ;
.lust as beaming cluster
On the silver surf.
Off they go ! Ye gods ! the ralt’.o
Of their hoofstrokes hum,
Like the roar of guns in battle
As they onward come.
In the van Althotas thunders,
Followed by Htlene ;
And old Bungey, working wonders,
Locked with Tridentine.
Marvel, Paris, Penance, Babel,
Alchemist and Thought,
Each going “ lemons ” for their stable
As each good one ought.
Wild Rose racing with Stromboli
Swings out on the right,
And each sportsman mutters, “Holy
Moses ! what a sight.”
In the centre of the streaming
Colours Florrie flies,
And St. Blaize’s jacket beaming
In the rear-guard lies.
Hero, Gladstone, Spy Glass racing
Close against the rails ;
Winifred Ducrow outpacing
Onward calmly sails;
Touch and go with Lady Edda,
Jammed inside the ruck,
And for Vain Hope, who had led her
Like a flying buck.
Round the home turn, swiftly speeding,
Racing come the field,
Bungebah, by two lengths leading,
Not an inch will yield.
Shamrock with a brilliant rattle,
Worthy of his birth,
Fighting gamely through the batlle
Reaches Bungey’s girth ;
Like a flash of summer lightning
Trieste comes away,
And each jockey’s grasp is tightening
Round his w r hip to-day.
Magic Circle on the railing
Claims supremacy;
And Wild Rose, with wet sheet sailing,
Ne’er will beaten be.
Down the straight the fight is bitter,
Gamest of the game.
Through the gleam of satin glitter,
Emblem of her name ;
Passing Shamrock without toying,
Collaring Trieste,
Down the straight the mare comes flying—
Wild Rose of the West.
Roar and yell, with accents tragic,
Round the buffet bar ;
Wild Rose, first-Trieste, and Magio
Circle won’t be far.
A. T. H. PRINSEP.
A RACY RHYME.
He needs must have observant eyes
Who means to spot the winning horse;
The Ma jic Circle's wondrous wise
In all the arts of spreading lies
And fatal tips around the course.
Tire hacker’s wits should never sleep,
Or fuddled be by many drinks —
Yet, wideawake a man may keep
The while he has a purpose deep
Jii going in tor Forty Winks.

Read
‘ -A- Golden Shanty.’
Josephus Riley.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rum was rich and rare,
There were wagers in the air,
Ihe atmosphere was rosy, and the longues were
wagging free ;
But one was in the revel
Whose occiput was level—
-1 lam Josephus Riley, from the North Countree.
The conversation’s flow
Was not devoid of “blow,”
And^ mother was it wanting in the plain, colloquial
With a most ingenuous smile—
. “ This here is not my style,”
omd plain Josephus Riley, from the North
Countree.
*' And I wouldn’t be averse
T° emptying my purse,
And laying some small wager with the present
companee,
To cut the matter short—
Boot-racing is my forte,”
oaid plain Josephus Rilev, from the North
Countree.
“I think it’s on the cards
ctm That I can run three hundred yards
(ihe match to be decided where vou gentlemen
agree)
Against your fleetest horse ;
The race would prove a source
Of pleasure,” said Josephus, from the North
Countree.
“ To equalise the task,
. This little start I ask—
-Ihe rider, ere he follows, must imbibe a cup of
tea;
A simple breakfast-cup
, He will have to swallow up
lhats me —Josephus Riley, from the North
Countree.”
Then a “ knowing ’un” looked wise,
. “ Begged to apologise,
But might he ask what temp’rature the liquid was
to be !
Would it come from out the pot,
it Milkless, steaming, boiling-hot ? ”
Oh, no, not at all,” said Riley, from the North
Countree.
“ Allow me to explain;
. I do observe, with pain,
This jocular reflection on my native honestee,
My bump of truth is huge,
I’d scorn a subterfuge”—
Said plain Josephus Riley, from the North
Countree.
“ Before the parties start
I’ll take the Judge apart
To prove, by tasting, whether I have tampered
wuth the tea;
And I beg to state again
_ Your suspicions give me pain,”
plain Josephus Riley, from the North
Countree.
Then they all were satisfied
That the match was “ boneefied,”
The bond wassigned, and Riley went to “preparate”
the tea;
But his slow, ambiguous smile
Would have seemed to token guile,
In any man but Riley, from the North Countree.
He brought the fatal cup
By its saucer covered up—
The Judge examined its contents with awful
gravitee,
Then read the papers o’er,
But could not find a flaw :
“Wade in! Josephus Riley, from the North
Countree. ”
Then the “wagerer ” just bowed,
And passing through the crowd,
He handed up the beverage unto the “ wageree ”;
And off across the flat,
Springing gayly, pit-a-pat,
Went plain Josephus Riley, from the North
Countree.
But behind him what a yell
Of execration fell
From lips that lent themselves to shapes of great
profanitee !
For the people of that town
Were done a lovely brown
By plain Josephus Riley, from the North
Countree.
And hei’e’s the reason why :
The tea was simply dry,
You might cat it, but to drink it was impossi-
bilitee;
But, curious to state,
Men did not appreciate
This hum’rous innovation from the North
Countree.
You’ll understand, of course,
That wager was a source
Of very little profit to the hapless “ wageree,”
And, dating from that day,
I much regret to say,
Men look askance at Riley, from the North
Countree.
BARCROFT H. BOAKE.
They have a female garroter in Melbourne.
She is a large, ample lady with a grip like a
patent claw-trap, and with bulging biceps, and a
rough but taking way. She lurks in lanes and
dark places, and lies low for fat men. Tat men
are her speciality. She likes a man with an
extensive oleaginous formation and an enlarged
bust, because he is soft and yielding and short-
winded and easy to choke; and, besides, a fat
man usually goes ornamented with a portly, pedal-
motion gold chronometer, and a quantity of petty
cash, and has diamond studs scattered over his
front, and carries incriminating documents in Ins
pockets that he will ransom handsomely rather
than have them fall into the hands of his confiding
wife. When the lady garroter starts to hug a
stertorous and respectable citizen of half-a ton
burden, and fondle him, and wheedle him out of
the price of a new hat and etcetras, she invariably
has luck, and the cumbrous elector goes home
with his eyes disjointed, and looking purple and
disconnected, and is content to be reviled as a
disgusting inebriate, rather than venture upon
explanations that will involve him in graver
suspicions, and make his home-life a burden to
him.

One account says that Williams’ defence
will be that his wives were addicted to singing
‘Little Annie Rooney” and “The Song that
Reached My Heart.”
From a late Melbourne decision it would
appear that when the hirer of time-payment furni-
ture falls into arrears the lender’s bailiffs cannot
legally break into the house and remove the goods,
even should there be a clause in the agreement
between hirer and borrower that such may be
done.
A lot may be forgiven Graham Berry,
because of his sensible treatment of the Victorian
aborigines while Chief Secretary. The superin-
tendent said they wouldn’t work. Berry declared
that they were only to work when they chose.
There were only 500 then—less now.
A sergeant of marines and a private
marine, the latter named Blatchford, slept in the
sfime room with others at Sydney; the sergeant,
according to his sworn evidence, saw Blatchford
fumbling about his bed in the night; in the morn-
ing, he missed a key, handkerchief, and shilling,
which were found on Blatchford, who, when
challenged, with apparent cheerfulness allowed
himself to be searched. Blatchford was then
charged With ,theft. Several officers of H.M.S.
Orlando.appeared iuCourtandgaveaccused,ayoung
man of 12 years service, a most excellent char
acter. Defendant, with much emotion, appealed
to Captain Fisher, the stipendiary magistrate, to
“ weigh carefully the facts of the case, and
endeavour to, see that he was a victim of circum-
stances.” The magistrate said there was a strong
doubt in the matter ; in consideration of Blatch-
ford’s good character, he would be discharged.
Of which decision the public will approve, because
unsupported circumstantial evidence is always to
be. distrusted, the square of distrust being in
inverse ratio to the apparent conclusiveness of the
circumstantial evidence.
The other day in a Sydney court the
customary Chinese witness was asked how he
would swear. He said cheerfully that lie would
swear anyway. He was asked next if he would
prefer to testify on a Bible or a lighted match, and
he answered effusively that either would do.
Then he was invited to express his views regarding
the subject of swearing upon a dead rooster, and
he said “ rooster welly good.” Somebody asked
him if he had any special views concerning the
Koran as an aid to conscience, and lie said it was
“ welly good, too.” The bench wanted to know
if he was a Buddhist and he responded “yes.”
Then another individual said that he believed the
witness, was a Christian, and being questioned he
said “yes” to this also. Finally a Bible and a
lighted match were both offered to him. Where-
upon he blew upon the Bible and tried to kiss the
match.' At this point the court asked him despair-
ingly where he would go to if he told what wasn’t
true and he said affably, “ alleeli.” Being furthei
asked inhere he would go to, ho answered “Yes.’
Then his evidence was taken, it being considered
that he was about as good a witness as tin
average. Probably lie was.
