A Much-Misunderstood Millionaire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
A tall man, solitary in his ways, always
dressed in grey tweed and invariably wearing a
soft felt hat. A healthy, ruddy complexion, iron-
grey beard and moustache, with extremely bright
blue eyes. These last are a noticeable feature and
compel your attention from their peculiar clear-
ness. When you pass this, individual, who is
almost certain to be alone, for. millionaires are a
lonely race, you have passed the richest man in
Australia, James Tyson, who has just come into
special prominence by the substantial financial
help he has accorded the 'Queensland.Government.
* # ;■
Ninety-nine out of every hundred people
pass “ Jimmy ” Tyson without noticing him, for
although everybody has heard of him, personally
he is little known. His dress does ’not betray
him, either by its loudly-proclaimed magnificence
or by any assumption of careless neglect. One
hears it often said that the mammoth millionaire
habitually clothes himself in slops or moleskin
and munches a roll and a polony for his lunch.
Nothing of the sort. His clothes are tailor-made
and well-fitting, though very sober in colour,' his
only singularity may be said to be his affection
for a soft felt hat as head-gear. He has liis club
and lunches there like other men.
* # #- -
Tyson belongs to a generation now giving
place to somewhat effete successors. He is one of
the early race of “ natives,” who first caused it to
be generally supposed that all Australians are
tall, gaunt, sun-burnt, and taciturn—-a race who
did good work in their day, and many of whom
are deservedly enjoying the fruits iff their early
struggles with the vyilderness. 1 1 'is true that
many who also struggled hard and honestly are
now m the Benevolent Asylums. s
James Tyson and his brothers vyfre born at
Menangle in N.S. Wales, and totfk an active
share in the pioneer settlement «©f Australia,
is the family failing; ‘One brother,
tmter, kept himself for years an outwardly poor
man, through his eagerness in buying up land with
every shilling he could muster; t)f the subject of
bnr sketch more yarns, fabulous arid otherwise,
have been told than of any other private indivi-
uual m Australia. Most of them areehestnuts
apphed indiscriminately to any rich man suspected
°i nearness,” and others are palpable lies.
Hen, is a fair specimen of the average Tyson
Before the present comfortable steamboat service
wSydney and Brisbane was in existence, it
sir j custom to charge passengers a fare for tran-
Af! anc * additionally for all meals taken on; board.
f,,„ r a while the arrangement was altered and the
hitl ? overe . ( * everything. Under the old system Tyson
with i,- en * n the habit of taking a supply of provisions
n cn , "ini, as he considered that the: charges made for
thes ° n h°. ai ’d were excessive. Between two of his trips
cllan ge in the arrangements were effected, but as he
", n °thing of it he took his stock of provisions on
him. i? 8 usua h The trip was a long one and Tyson found
/ ou t of rations with Sydney Heads many miles
vvftnu V. tfe called the steward and asked him what he
renlv <i^ ve . *° P a V f° r a meal. “Nothing,” was the
sniZui ’t a " included in your ticket.” Tyson was
for hr i*l w ‘th disgust, and made up as well as he could
ms lost opportunities during the remaining meals.
Now, as a matter of fact,-no such arrangement
‘. ''barging separately for meals ever existed on
l a mers running between Sydney and Queens-
We have no wish to pourtray king-monopolist
Tyson in the light of a saint, ready for a halo and
enshrinement in a stained-glass window; but so
many of the stories concerning him are greatly
misleading as to his true character, that a few
veritable facts about the chief millionaire of Aus-
tralia may prove entertaining. James Tyson loves
the bush, is essentially a bushman, and is always
more conversational on subjects relating to country
affairs. The only time the writer of this article
ever had a lengthy talk with him was in Brisbane,
just after returning from a prolonged trip out
West. The conversation turned upon hardship
and short commons in new country, and Tyson
grew quitd eloquent on the various animals and
vermin that could be used to support life after the
ordinary rations had failed. He expressed a
decided disapproval of ’possums; bandicoot was
what he swore by.
Tyson’s objection to being imposed upon, and
his unostentatious ways have gained him an un-
deserved reputation for stinginess, but too many
well-known instances of his generosity and grati-
tude' (which -latter examples almost invariably
take the form of liberal aid to men in difficulties
whom he knows to be resolute and honest) are
extant for this to be the case. But he is obstin-
ately determined not to be “had as a certain
church dighitary in Queensland found to his cost.
The gentleman in question was pushing a charity
forward; and .Tyson sent him.a cheque for ,£250.
The reverend*"sent back a polite note thanking
him fpr the donation, but intimating that he had
hoped for a larger sum from a man of Mr. Tyson’s
known wealth. Tyson, in reply, asked for the
cheque back. It was readily sent, the clergy-
man smilingly expecting a larger one. It never
came. Tyson was disgusted at such barefaced
begging; and now that parson never omits an
opportunity of assuring his hearers that a rich
man cannot get into heaven.
It is related of him that he always travels by
steamer under an assumed name, after the manner
of Jonah Perkins, in order to avoid any possible
application for a loan ; but it is not the case, any
more than the hackneyed story that when travel-
ing in the bush lie carries a few cobs of corn in his
saddle-pouch, and camps out and boils them in his
quart-pot in preference to putting up at a station
or a roadside inn.
Tyson’s peculiar habits, unconventional, solitary
and sparing of speech as he is—by the way, he in-
variably calls everybody “ Mister,” no matter how
long he has known him—have been the cause of
much of this misconception. He is supposed to
be a woman-hater, but people who lived out on the
Warrego during the early days tell a different
story, and though our multi-millionaire has never
been guilty of the weakness of matrimony and
absolutely shudders at the restraints of fashionable
society he will,.tell you, if he knows you well
enough, that he yields to no one in his admiration
(from a respectable distance, certainly) of the cul-
tured and healthy and cheerful woman who is, or
is fitted to become, a prudent, helpful and virtuous
housewife. It must be said, in extenuation of,
James Tyson’s bachelorhood, that he is the'only
one of his family against whom there is anything
like the shadow of a prima-facie charge of
misogyny. No jury would convict his brother
Peter op such an accusation. . Nor his now illus-
trious nephew James.
Coming down in the steamer from Brisbane to
Sydney, B , a well-known and genial Queens-
land squatter, shared the cabin with Tyson. As a
rule, the confined space of a cabin necessitates one
man dressing at a time.
. B— 7- was dressing, and Tyson lying, medita-
ting, in his bunk.
“-B ,” he said, at last, “ you are not half the
man you were. You are getting like my nephew,
J-—j too fat; you eat too much, and drink too
much. Why don’t you live naturally ?”
“Do you believe in living naturally ?” said B .
“ Certainly I do.’’
“ Then get married as soon as you can,” replied
B ,as he left the cabin. Tyson’s reflections on
the subject have not been recorded.
The same man, who was a neighbour of Tyson’s,
relates that, being in want of a sheep-drover, an
individual, whom we may call Blanks, applied for
the billet, and mentioned Tyson as a former em-
ployer. On being applied to, the masterful James
gave the following reference : —“ Blanks ! yes.
He took some sheep down for me, and did very
well with them. I was giving him thirty shillings
a week. I raised him to two pounds and gave
him another lot. He did all right, but not quite
so well as the first time, and I noticed that he
commenced to wear a collar. However, he got
another lot, and a rise of ten shillings. This time
he did not do so well as before, and he had started
a necktie with a pin in it. Well, Mister, to make
a long story short, he got another job and another
rise, and then I saw him buckling on a pair of
long-hooked spurs, and he had about four pouches
on his belt, but he didn’t do anything like so well.
No, Mister.’ Blanks is a first-class drover at thirty
shillings a week, but at two or three pounds he’s
no good. Success spoils him.”
In the sixties, when a race meeting was being
held in Hay, Tyson passed the place. He camped
out, and his horses wandered away. Next morn-
ing B met him with his saddle and swag, on
his shoulder, going down: the main street. “Why
don’t you stop and wait for your horses?” he
asked. “No,” returned the stubborn James, “I
never turnback.” He walked on eighteen miles
before the black boy overtook him with the lost
horses
An instance of alleged nearness has often been
told regarding one of his western stations,
whereon, it is asserted, he refused to allow any
milkers to be broken in, on the ground that every
pound of butter made and every quart of milk
drunk robbed the calves of flesh.' The truth and
origin of the yarn being that, visiting the place
during a severe drought, he found a number of
poor milkers and half-starved,.calves kept in at a
time when the paddocks were destitute of grass,
and naturally ordered them to be turned out.
All the money he has made in pastoral matters
has been acquired through good judgment and
shrewd management. Long ago, when fat cattle
brought a decent price in Melbourne, he had
secured good fattening paddocks in one of the
best districts of Victoria, and regularly over-
landed his store bullocks and had some always
ready to secure top prices in the Melbourne
market.
His method, and love of care, pursue him
through all the avocations of his life, as witness
hjs.careful remembrance of the fact that he owed
the barmaid of a leading Sydney hotel ninepence.
After an absence of nearly twelve months, he
duly called in to pay it. Not to be beaten, the
girl handed him a pair of socks which she asserted
he had left behind after his visit, and which she
had had washed and darned.' Naturally Tyson
took them with unmoved gravity or he would not
have, been Tyson.
One day when returning home on one of his
Queensland stations he overtook a couple of swag-
men, who asked him the distance, to the home-
stead, which he told' them. “It belongs to that
hungry , Tyson, doesn’t it ?” said one. “ Any
chance of a feed there?” “You’ll get a night’s
shelter,” said Tyson, riding on, when one caljbd
out, “ Got any matches or tobacco on youi:?”
Tyson had both, although he does not smoke fall
the same, it is his delight to cut up tobacco,|jat
which he sniffs lovingly, as if he did indulge in the
weed). He gave the men a fig of tobacco and
some matches. At sundown the swagmen turrjed
up and the storekeeper gave them the usual
rations. Just as he had finished Tyson came;ln
to the store and remarked, “ These men owe me
6d.-—6d. for tobacco, and 2d. for matches.” h
“ Who the carnation are you ?”
“I’m that hungry , Tyson,” he replied.
“ If you had been civil you might have had tjjie
tobacco and welcome, but now you can pay for
it.” And they did. lj
Tyson’s first step towards fortune was made, he
relates, far back in the Fifties. He was taking
stock over to Victoria and found himself stuck bp
by flood and bog. Near him a sheep-drover was
camped under similarly wretched circumstances,
and after soine talk Tyson bought up his sheepijat
Gs. per head. The purchase completed, he set
about findingmeansof transitaoross the flooded river
and obtaining a suitable-boat succeeded in crossing
both his own cattle and the sheep without lobs.
He made for Bendigo, then ip its glory, where'he
sold the sheep (16,000) at 265. per head.- and the
cattle at GOs.-pcr cwt. Since then, by judicidiis
investments, and a ready grasp of every situation
where there was an opportunity for a good bar-
gain, ho has made the colossal fortune with which
he is generally credited. His capital is variously
estimated at anything between four and ten
millions, and it is stated on good authority thipt
one year’s operations in Queensland and New
South Wales netted him at least £425.000. This
is a tidy income, and it is not to be wondered (it
that bis fame lias travelled beyond Australian
shores, and-that he was once made the .subject bf
an article in a London paper, wherein a lady
trotted him out as a matrimonial prize worth
trying for.
With the family eye on land, Tyson has bp|h
interested in syndicates for land-grant railways in
Queensland on several occasions, but, as the pro-
jected lines have never come off, his estate is still
lacking the ample dimensions he would seem to
desire. What will become of his wealth is a
question. He has no relative who has any great
claim on him, and he is a hale, hardy man of 60,
with every faculty unimpaired, and as keenly bent
on making money as ever, so that no one can
estimate what his fortune may be when his time
comes at last.
He has been heard to lament on several occasions
that his tastes did not lie in some direction outside
of stock and finance. Even horse-racing would
be.welcome if he could only bring himself to take
a thorough interest in it. " But the bent of ms
disposition does not tend that way and he has Jto
content himself with turning over a few paltry
hundreds of thousands every year. A millionaire
who does not drink, does not smoke, is imperviopa
to the most fascinating wiles of fascinating womeip;
and yet is not a sordid miser of tW Daniel Dander
type, is something unique, and Australia may tike
credit for having produced the fivlt .specimen'df
the kind. He has not .even bailtliimfelf a “lordfy
pleasure house ” after the faMifdn of Mount
Morganaires or Broken "Millionaires, one of whom
has lately had designed a dwelling-house >vh|ch jla
a conundrum. The puzzled spectator on seeing
this design says, ‘‘ O.ycs. ji.jic.w.tuwiclialll’.- “ A

new theatre!” “A lunatic asylum!” until at
last he is told that it is a simple dwelling-house,
and is struck speechless. Tyson is too much of a
genuine bushman to feel any pleasure in such
empty magnificence. Ho dislikes society. Go-
vernment House has never had any attractions for
him. Even the “genial” Carington could not
get the reserved and silent Jimmy to put his legs
underneath his mahogany, although he tried per-
sonal persuasion and Lady C. wrote an invitation
in her own fair hand.
As already remarked, Tyson is one of the old
race of natives who grew up to self-reliance and
hard work. Their paths were not made easy for
them by a rum-selling progenitor, or a successful
land-booming parent, but they learnt their bush-
craft from Mother Nature, and have been her
children ever since. To this may be ascribed the
fact that in spite of Tyson’s business shrewdness
and natural disposition towards making money,
he retains his love of simplicity, of fresh, breezy
nature, and the acquirement of knowledge of ail
sorts. An indefatigable reader, he has probed
problems with which no outsider would ever
suspect him of meddling.
Once, during his early days, he was driving a
horse-dray, and at the foot of a hill the horses,
which were pretty well done, stuck him up. A
teamster was passing, and from him Tyson asked
the usual favour of a pull up the pinch. The
driver, seeing he was in a fix, asked a price which
Tyson justly considered extortionate, so he
declined to give it. He unloaded the dray single-
handed, and by dint of hard work and cunning
zigzagging, got it to the top of the hill; then he
got the load up piece-by-piece, and finally led the
knocked-up horses up and proceeded triumphantly
down the other side, having successfully resisted
an attempt at imposition.
One evening a well-known bush-missionary (self
dubbed) came to one of Tyson’s stations when he
happened to be at home. The individual sent in
hi* name and was received with the usual bush
hospitality. He improved the occasion after
di iner and extracted from Tyson a half-promise
th »t he would contribute towards his little mission.
Unfortunately there was some rum on the place,
and, at the invitation of the manager, the “ser-
vant of the Lord ” took two or three second-
mates’ nips that put him to sleep in one of the
verandah-chairs. Next morning he reminded
Tyson of his application for a subscription, but
that individual grimly remarked that as it was a
dry country, the kindest thing he could do was to
give the shepherd the perpetual free run of the
tank —the water-tank, not the rum-keg.
We can scarcely say that it would be well for
the country if there were many Tysons in it, but
there is no doubt that the same sagacity and
earnestness, the same splendid generalship and
iron determination which have served him so well
at money-making, would have brought him to the
front rank in many other pursuits. His good deeds
are little known, for he does not advertise them.
Now and again one hears of them by accident, but
everybody is ready to repeat some alleged anec-
dote of his dog-mean closefistedness.
A Strange Symposium,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bourke-streefc is very busy to-day. Up
and down it flows a living river—strangest of
rivers-with two currents which run in opposite
directions at the same time. Looking up at the
Post-office Clock, with its face in half-mourning
for lost time, I find that the hour is 11 a.m.
I have an appointment to meet a man at a certain
place in five minutes. In five minutes I am there.
This is the nearest hint I can afford to give as to
the position of the hotel wherein I first met the
many curious characters who form the Symposium
I am about to attempt to describe.
A shady, spacious room, with a suggestion of
green coolness in the atmosphere. The suggestion
comes from long-fronded ferns and large-leaved
geraniums that grow in tubs, also painted green,
outside a window which looks into the back-yard.
Coming out of the glitter of the garish street into
this dim chamber behind the bar, one has to
pause a moment before being able to make out
what it contains. But as the eyes grow accus-
tomed to the half-light the following objects
become visible : two tables, two sofas, two spit-
toons, a number of chairs, and a mirror in a fly-
speckled frame hanging at an inebriate angle over
the mantelpiece. Each sofa supports a semi-
recumbent form, and most of the chairs are occu-
pied. There are long glasses on the tables, some
of them entirely empty, and some with a resi-
duum of viscous froth at the bottom. Beer is
evidently the favourite beverage in this boudoir
of Bacchus —and beer in long glasses, too. There
is not a small, or even medium-sized, tumbler on
the table. An ominous sign, this.
# * #
Why an ominous sign ? Because when a
man has come to the stage in his carousing career
when he wants the largest quantity of liquor that
can be got for the smallest silver coin in currency,
his palate is ruined, he no longer knows the fine
luxury of rolling a rare brand on his tongue, with
his head thrown slightly backward and his eyes
cast thankfully in the direction of the ceiling.
There is as much difference between him and the
man whose delicate senses are touched to
tremulous delight by the flavour and aroma of
noble vintages as between the bee that sips honey
from the cup of the flower, and the ox that
devours it in one bite, petals, pistils, leaves, stalk
and all. He is, in fact, nothing more than a
human hogshead whose sole ambition is to be full,
and always full.
* * *
The person who made the appointment
with me has left word with the siren behind the
bar that he was called away on important busi-
ness, but will be back in the course of an hour. I
am going to sit down and wait for him, and, in
the meantime, make what observations I can, in
a quiet way, of what goes on around me.
# * *
To begin with, I notice that most of the
regular members of the Symposium are present.
By regular members I mean those who make
their appearance in this room day after day and
spend much time there. They are as lavish in
this sort of expenditure as though they were
clockmakers. The Symposiarch is in his usual
seat with his back to the mirror in the fly-speckled
frame. His usual oxybaphon of “colonial” ale
stands on the table, within convenient reach of his
hand. I call him the Symposiarch because he is
perpetual president of this informal club—the
first to come and the last to go, and never—unless
on a fishing expedition or laid up with the gout—
a day out in his attendance. I have a fancy that
he is included in the lease of the premises. Hour
after hour in the early part of the day he sits
silent in his chair, absorbed and absorbing, his
head in a cloud of smoke and his feet on each
side of a spittoon. But, later on, after he has
emptied many oxybaphons, he awakes from his
lethargy—and becomes amusing. His humour is
of the dry order—in fact, I may say that I have
not met with none drier. It needs a vast amount
of moisture before it blossoms. Even then it is
too Rabelaisian to bo quotable in a refined and
modest age like the present. Some of his “good
stories” if printed in a paper would increase its
circulation by thousands in one week, and pro-
bably send its proprietor to gaol the week after.
# # #
mi • .
The most taciturn man in the room is the
individual who is sitting in the darkest corner,
wide ; eyed and wakeful, but speechless and move-
less, like a red-nosed image of the god Harpo-
crates. And yet this man could if he chose tell a
more thrilling story of strange experiences he has
had, ranging from piracy in the Aru Archipelago
down to newspaper-editing in the Ear North, than
any of the wild and weird school of romance-
writers now in vogue has yet given to the world,
I know this because he told me the story one day
when we happened, by curious chance, to be the
only persons in the room. It is a pity that he is
not a romance-writer himself. But possibly he is.
He may have been trying on me the effect of a
few chapters from his forthcoming work, like the
man in one of Aldrich’s sketches who told to a
sympathising stranger a terrible yarn about being
shut up in a family vault for an hour, and coming
out with his hair as white as snow at the end of
the time, and, when he had worked up the feelings
of the stranger to a satisfactory pitch of horror,
confessed that the yarn had no foundation, in fact,
but was simply his next contribution to the
magazine for which he was writing. I would be
sorry to think that my friend in the corner was
guilty of such baseness, but I would not be greatly
surprised as I distrust all silent men. If a man is
not talking he must be plotting, and plotting no
good, you may be sure. The Governments of the
world are not inimical to secret societies for
nothing.
* # *
Yet, for all the conversation that is carried
on this room the party gathered in it might as
well be a Symposium in the Catacombs. Apathy
sits like a succubus on the chests of all of them,
and the air is dense and deadening with invisible
husks of physical and mental decay. One begins,
after sitting here for a while, to lose grasp of
existence and to wonder in a vagne, vegetable wav
how much longer the world will have to wait for
the Resurrection. And all this time, a few yards
off, the river of life runs full and fast in the sunlit
street outside. But this is a stagnant backwater
in which you will find only battered hulks and
rotting derelicts. Brave and goodly vessels in
their time, too, many of them, taut and trim and
well-provided for their destined vovages—and
now ! What has brought them to this sad condi-
tion ? God knows—and the brewers. Woman
also, no doubt, in some cases.
: ’ #
Over there on the sofa under the window
lies, perhaps, the saddest wreck of all. A man of
not more than 40 years of age but who looks as
though he were 60. Many years ago he made
some reputation by writing a few things which
were considered full of fine promise. He even
published a book, and the critics in reviewing it
said that the true Elizabethan drama had arisen
from its grave at Stratford-on-Avon, and taken
shape, and colour, and life again, under the
Southern Cross. This, with the flattery of his
friends, was more than the head of the poor author
could stand. It was a case of tete montee with
him from that time forth. He despised the every-
day drudgery of the press and, as there was not a
steady demand for tragedies—by the way, the
first and only edition of the one for which he had
obtained so much praise, was subsequently sold
by weight—he went steadily to the dogs and the
devil. Yet this is not altogether true. He made
many gallant struggles ere he—though hardly
with their excuse of genius, if genius be an excuse
a matter which I am very much inclined to
doubt—
r
The late W. B. Dailey used to tell a
story of the last time he saw Deniehy, who was
then living, or dreaming that he was living, in
Bathurst. He found the brilliant but erratic
essayist in an obscure back-parlour of an obscure
hotel, sitting upright on a chair and gazing
intently into vacancy. He spoke to him, but
could only get a nod of recognition in reply,
“ Can I do anything for you, Dan ? ” said Dailey.
“ Call for some brandy,” said Deniehy.
“Bring in two glasses of brandy,” remarked the
late Privy Councillor, turning to somebody belong,
ing to the hotel who was standing close by.
“ Stop ! ” exclaimed Deniehy, holding his thin,
long hand up, “ Two glasses! Bring in a bottle!”
The bottle was brought in and he poured him-
self out half a tumblerful, which he drank off raw
at one gulp. Then he poured out a similar
quantity again, and drank it off also. “And
then,” Mr. Dailey used to say, “ he talked for an
hour on end with the tongues of men and angels,
And then he suddenly collapsed, and I couldn’t
get another w r ord out of him.”
# # #
* 5O 1S ’ chough in a much smaller degree,
of course, with the man on the sofa. After he
has taken a certain number of drinks, not too
P an 7 but just enough to produce a transient blaze
in his smouldering brain, he grows eloquent over
m &ny things, but chiefly over what splendid poems
and stories he is going to write when I can give
U P> P oin^in PT with gloomy scorn at his beer-
& ass? an<A emptying it the next moment. It is a
s °rrowful sight but you begin to weary of it
when y? u se f more than a dozen times,
~ Another character in this curious gathering is
, 9 ne - ar m ed man with the cadaverous com-
P, ex , 10 , n , a ? d ff re y-black beard, who sits at
FJ 16 ,^ a blc furthest from the door, bolt upright, as
“ ~\ e we . re a wax figure. He is not, however;
neither is he dead. He is merely waiting for
somebody to ask him to take a drink, and, every
now and then, casts a wistful glance m the direc-
tion of the door. But no succour comes from that
quarter, and he closes his eyes again with a look
aa mtly resignation, and goes on waiting. He
is the most patient man I ever saw. The others
call him the Vampire—why, I know not, unless it
is in reference to his ghastly colour. But he is
distinct from them in one respect, at least. He is
Plunged deep down in the depths abysmal,
The gulf, and grave, of Maginn, and Burns,
And pawned his soul for the devil’s dismal
Stock of returns.

, h ol |iy m an in therdom who ig not fitted-up with
a drink-discoloured nose. The nose-of the Sym-
Lwiarch is of a dead: purple,-hue—a> fact which
Ked little Litnley, a minor light of the Sym-
posium (he is inot able,' to attend the * meetings
regularly, on account of having to go to the

ehest examined), to tell hirh one day that “if he

# # *
Hark !
Is this the face that launched a thousand ships
And burned the topless towers of Ilium ?
That’s little Spavinson, the veterinary surgeon,
addressing the barmaid. He is a great Eliza-
bethan scholar, and conducts most of his conver-
sation through the medium of quotations from his
favourite authors. They say, indeed, that he
soothes the horses that are brought to him for
treatment with scraps from Shakespeare and Mas-
singer, and, when one of them is inclined to turn
restive, hauls him up with “Marlowe’s mighty
line> ” * * #
There is a sudden stir among the dry
bones as he enters. Dull eyes brighten, and limp
figures become erect as if by magic. The wistful
look has left the Vampire’s eye, and given place
to gratitude for what he is about to receive. The
gentleman on the other sofa, whom I have not yet
described, rises up suddenly, alert and smiling.
He is a pretty picture. His clothes are covered
with a thousand beer-stains, stain upon stain —a
palimpsest of stains—so that by analysing them
you could tell what he has had to drink every day
year past, as Flaubert says you could divine
the mnners that Madame Bovary had cooked for
a Whole twelvemonth previously by the grease-
spots on her gown. Even the Remains of the
Tragic Poet became spasmodically alive, because
Spavinson is a man who always has money and is
always ready to spend it in the Symposium. He
is, in fact, a leading member of it himself, and
certainly the most solvent of the crowd.
# # *
Then the dumb find a voice— two or three
voices, you would fancy, judging by the Babel
that ensues. • After a time—it is always the way
with people who have missed or thrown away
their opportunities in the present—they begin to
talk about their past, glories. Listen to one of
them—it is the man with the beer-stains who is
talking:—“l retnember one day the Governor
telling Tom —his body-servant, you know—to ask
the head-keeper—the Governor had six keepers—
if there was a box in the Hoggleyhole Cover,
because Lord Chumley Cheroot —you’ve heard of
him, "Of course, fine sportsman and great friend of
the Governor’s —and the Marquis of Mngford—
another great friend of the Governor’s—were
coming down next day. The Governor, you
know, kept a pack of hounds, and used to hunt
the county—”
■ “ Were they the Pytchley or the Quorn?” sar-
castically enquires another highly - connected
gentleman, stroking a nose that ought to blister
his fingers. He is wild to get a chance to “ chip
in with reminiscences of the magnificent style in
which he himself was brought up.
But here is my friend, punctual to his appoint*
ment. One word, however, before I finish. Let
no reader of this slight sketch conclude that the
men I have been describing are mean men. On
the contrary, there are none more generous when
they have the money. And that is precisely the
cause of all the trouble and the raison d'etre of
the Strange Symposium.
OBEEVE KOE.
Dear Bulletin, —The following veracious
yarn will illustrate the danger of neglecting the
early education of children : —About the year ’B5
there lived on the tipper Ooomera a married
couple known to the settlers by the name of
Hooley. One season the flat on which they lived
became infested with bandicoots ; the little pests
tore up Hooley’s garden stuff, and, to use his own
words, “played the very devil wid him.” In an
evil hour a friend advised Hooley to get a gun and
shoot some of his enemies. He did. He got a
gun—a discarded regulation musket —with powder
and shot to match. Hooley didn’t know much
about guns, so onedayhe essayed to “practish”
with his musket. Having loaded up carefully, he
set his back hard against a fence, pointed the
musket at a gum-tree, and pulled the trigger.
Bang she went, with a roar like a nine-pounder,
but not a scratch on the tree, “Not enough
powdher,” remarked Hooley, “I’ll thry a little;
more,” and he emptied about half a teacupful of
diamond-grain down the neck of his artillery.
Bang again. Hooley staggered, recovered him-
self, and his eye again sought “ruin and devasta-
tion ” on the bark of the tree. Again not a scratch.
“ Gimme the powdher again, Biddy!” he yelled,
and he seized the flask and dosed the much-
enduring musket again; Heaven knows what
quantity he put in. There was a terrific report
and a yell as the musket kicked Hooley on the
jaw, and knocked - him over the fence, and
climbed over after, him. and kicked him on
the other jaw, and then jumped on him, and hit
him below the belt; then seeking more worlds to
conquer it tore up three rods of split fence, and
chased Biddy 11 times around the milking yard,
after which it swam the river and rushed into the
office of an electioneering J.P. and broke up 15
dead marines, upset the nail-can and gin-case
comprising the office furniture, tore the big X
comprising the J.P.’s official signature off some
important documents and swallowed the docu-
ments in question, and finally burst, filling the air
with gun, and devastated J.P., and other ruins.
Hooley is buried on the bank of the river. His
»ad end is a solemn warning to parents and
guardians. If in his neglected youth he had been
taught to shoot landlords he would have known
how to load a gun properly, and would not have
expended himself m a futile attempt to shoot a
g mntree without putting son e shot in his gun
along witli the powder. —Yours,
&c., G. B.
A Large Machinery Warehouse
From a “needle to an anchor is a trite .expres-
sion which is seldom to be found more Completely
exemplified than in an inspection of, the machinery
and hardware warehouses of Messrs. H. G.
Watkins & Co., North Kent-stree't, Sydney.
This firm’s extensive stores are situated opposite
the Flagstaff, and occupy a prominent position as
seen from the harbour. Notwithstanding thftt
Sydney .has its immense importing warehouses,
and is justly, noted for the enterprise of its mer-
chants, yet the buyer generally finds it impossible
to obtain all his requirements under one roof. It
has, however, apparently been the desire of
Messrs. R. G. Watkins & Co. to obviate this
difficulty, and recompense their visitorsfordrawing
them up that end of the town, past the allurements
of the Grosvenor Hotel, into their warehouse,
where it was our privilege to see a display which
is certainly unequalled in the Australian’colonies,!
and we doubt even if'suehra varied assortment can
be found concentrated in the old country. In a
building extending some 200 ft. along Rent-street
there has been laid down a solid floor, and in the
first division is exhibited a display of hew
machinery, most of.-which is by the'rehowned'fitm
of Eliza Tinsley—also Isaac Storey and Sons’
engine-fittings, Evans and Sons’ . steam'-pUmp'a,
and a variety of general wood-working tools,--gas
and steam engines, &c. Thii department CQtn-
prises every requirement for the mining manager,
engineer, contractor, or sawmill proprietor,
while the value represented cannot be . less,
than £20,000. In course of conversation Mr. 4
Watkins explains that his business'’' supplies
the principal railway contractors of this colony,|
and that he takes an active interest
these contracts, but not in a way That.such fa (
statement might he accepted unless explained,
he does, by saying that he keeps; himself - keenly,
alive to their wants, imports the. best and
tools and labour-saving implements,*ahdihqldtjhpi
opinion that no firm can properly/ cater.|or^con-,
tractor’s requirements and at the same.tbue com-,
pete with them by tendering for
works. The support accorded to the
to bear out this theory, and no
kins’s experience in this trade
for the high position he occupiesin the estimation
of contractors. . .
On passing into the second division of the
establishment we found it stocked con-
ceivable description of heavy plant and machinery
—iron, steel, tubes, oils, &c., and powerful cranes
are utilised to transport the various heavy pack-
ages to and fro the whole range of the store. - An-
item of interest struck us in the original Beam
Engine which did duty for the S.M.}HEKAm)_many
years since, still looking as fresh as the day it was
turned out of its maker’s hands. Alongside ythis
in chronological order we were invited to inspect
the 16 h.p. Otto Gas Engine which supplanted the
Beam Engine and supplied the motive power for
the same journal until a few month's since when
another type of engine was put in to replace it.
Surrounding these in a covered space of about two
acres there are ranged portable and other engines
of every size and type —boilers, batteries, winding-
gears, amalgamators and pumps of all kinds
and capacities from 500 to 60,000 gallons
per hour. Mr. Watkins modestly . ven-
tured to say that he advertises having the most
complete stock in Australia, and at this stage we
are not prepared to combat that statement, but we
are agreeable to compliment him on his pluck and
enterprise in providing such a stock. No doubt,
as we have indicated, there is a difficulty in find-
ing firms who cater for all mechanical require-
ments, but it would be almost impossible to call
for an item which would not be immediately
forthcoming in this warehouse. There will shortly
be very proper legislation for the supervision of
land boilers, and Mr. Watkins expresses his con-
viction that it would do away with the pernicious
sale of death-traps in the shape of second-hand
boilers, and he always declines to sell, other than
new steel boilers tested to 150 or 200 pounds
per square inch, of which he stocks,a large
number. Visitors from the country,’ especially
those of a mechanical turn of mind, when tired of
the National Gallery, Museum, and “Our Har-
bour,” could profitably spend a few hours of
interest and enjoyment at Messrs. R. G. Watkins
& Co.’s Warehouse, and we feel sure that those
who do will be courteously received, and will
agree with us that the firm has supplied “a long-
felt want. ” . .
The important point of news copyright is
to come before the London courts in a week or
two, over the case of the Times v Hansard.
Hansard slashes out whole speeches from the
Times, and there being a change in the Hansard
proprietary, the Times seizes the occasion to ,try
the point; We have shown how cable news ,is
copyright in Australia, or in Australian cities,
but how long it extends is the question.

To Readers of “ The Bulletin.”
“Every man can write at least one.book,” every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, orhallads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better. Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived by The Bulletin.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
More than five-and-twenty years ago, on a newly-
formed station on a north-western Queensland
river, a young fellow, named Hewett, was left in
charge, in consequence of the managing partner
having been hastily called down to Sydney. It was
a cattle station on wide-spreading plains, backed
up by patches of scrubby forest. You could ride for
miles and miles without seeing an elevation of
any sort, or anything livelier than a soaring
kite. Naturally, time hung rather heavily on the
hands of Hewett and his men, and, consequently,
the loose morality of the time and district was
adopted, and although the blacks were not “let
in,” as it is termed, several native women were
camped at the station. However, that was thought
no more of then than it is at present in some
parts of Western Australia.
As things turned out it was nearly three years
before Hewett was relieved from duty. Then the
station passed into the hands of the bank, and one
of their own men came up and took charge.
Amongst the half-caste children about the place
was one bright little fellow who was a great pet
with them all, especially his father, Hewett. He
had a strange white tuft of hair on his head,
which the men used to call “ Pompey’s brand ; ”
he having been named Pompey. Hewett left and
Pompey continued to grow up on the station.
Twelve or thirteen years passed, and by one of
those common freaks of fortune, Hewett returned
to take permanent charge of the same station in
the interests of a different owner, it having
change hands once more.
Naturally, everything was somewhat altered.
The cattle had increased, the improvements were
better, and everything more civilised. One thing,
however, was unchanged—the old racial feud with
the blacks continued, and a native-police barracks
was established on the river. Hewett’s successor,
it seems, had tried the experiment of allowing the
blacks to come in and camp on the river without
interference, and although it gave the men some
trouble with the cattle for a time, it answered
well, apparently.
However, the usual outbreak at length took
place. The natives very soon took to killing cattle
again. Then they were hunted out once more,
and with them went all the quiet gins £nd
piccaninies who had been camped at the station.
When Hewett again took charge he found he had
plenty to do to keep the natives out of the herd.
Just before he came back, one of the old hands,
of his former time, had been re-engaged, an old
fellow known as Ben. Walker.
At last, going out on the run one day to look at
a camp of fat cattle that were shortly intended
for market, they were encountered by a panting
lot of bullocks with hanging tongues and heaving
sides. Little need to guess what had happened ;
the blacks had been amongst them and the cattle
would be fit for nothing for months. Sending the
others to collect and steady what they could find,
Hewett and old Ben rode on to the police-camp.
The officer in charge was at home, and with a
couple of native troopers they were soon on their
way to the camp. The fresh tracks were picked
up and the pursuit commenced. It was pretty
easy tracking, and by dusk they had settled the
site of the natives’ camp, and arranged to “ dis-
perse ” it at daylight.
Day broke and the camp was surrounded before
the blacks, who are always late sleepers, had time
to fly. Hewett was after a couple who were
dodging; him in the scrub. Suddenly one, who
was quite a youngster, and had a spear in his
hand that he had picked up in his flight, turned
and faced him, out of breath. Hewett raised his
revolver and took aim, when the glint of the early
morning sun fell straight on the nigger. It acted
like a flash of memory. He saw by the yellowish-
brown nude figure that the boy was a half-caste,
and recognised by the white forelock, “ Pompey’s
brand,” that it was his own son he was going to
kill. The young fellow saw him hesitate, took
courage, and sent the spear straight through
Hewett’s body. The clutching finger pulled the
trigger, and Pompey went to the happy hunting-
ground with his father’s bullet in his brain.
The officer and Ben Walker rode up soon after-
wards, dismounted hastily and examined Hewett,
but he was on the point of death. They did all
they could to ease him, and then Ben went to look
at the nigger. “By God !” he said, as he looked
at the white tuft, now partly blood-stained, “ if
it isn’t Pompey!’’
“Who’s Pompey ?” asked the officer who was
just behind him,
“ His son,” said Ben shortly, pointing at the
dead body of the white man.
A certain shoemaker in Melbourne
seriously thinks of indicting a well-to-do family
for getting boots mended under false pretences.
He had fallen in love with one Mary Jane, a
domestic servant, and he used to mend that fair
enslaver’s boots gratis. Mary Jane’s mistress
knew of his existence—in fact she had met him in
the kitchen, and been introduced to him when he
was cornered and couldn’t get away in time, and
she also knew of the boot arrangement. After
this introduction the bootmaking youth found
that Mary Jane’s hoof-cases came round to be
repaired a good deal oftener than before, but he
suspected no evil, and patched them without
murmuring. Then they began to come faster still
—in fact, Mary got a pair mended every second
week, and it occurred to him that she must be
heavy about the feet and dreadfully rough on shoe-
leather. Still it was a delicate question, and he
didn’t like to hint to her that she would be an
expensive girl to keep shod. The next thing that
puzzled him was the strange discrepancies in the
sizes—apparently Mary had feet that opened and
shut like a telescope and could fit any size from
three to eight. He stood that also, but it worried
him a good deal. But when at last the usual
small boy brought round a pair of men’s No. 10’s
he struck, and fled to his sweetheart’s kitchen in
search of an explanation. And then Mary Jane
repudiated all the boots, and he discovered that
he had been trepanned into repairing the hoof-
boxes of the entire family without remuneration—
and, as already mentioned, he wants to know
whether he can have that family arrested on a
charge of having boots repaired under false
pretences.

“Birds in their little nests agree.” Not
always, judging from the Review of Reviews’
extract from an article on “Marriage among
birds”: —“Sometimes the female stork will con-
spire with a young paramour to kill her husband,
just as if she had been a human being.” We
have often noticed the storks in the Zoo playing
at what wo thought was “ follow the leader.” But
we now conclude that it was Joseph Stork running
away from Mrs. Potiphar Stork.
It is a gross scandal that, in a certain
important Australian mining town, all the local
papers are now more or less under the control of a
firm who now bull and bear local mining stock at
their own sweet unchecked will, and float wild-
cat companies, and play the mischief with all
their opponents. In addition to this, they are
close friends with the reigning political party, and
barefacedly distort and misrepresent local public
opinion, besides having secured the powerful
prejudiced favour of the Attorney-General of the
day.
It is estimated that every one of the Mel-
bourne Associated Banks has received and lent
out again about six times its own capital. One
instance; A bank’s paid-up capital and reserve-
funds amount to something over <£2,000,000 ; its
deposits are .£13,100,000; it has lent out
<£13,750,000, besides having a few millions of notes
in currency ! One-fourth of its “ capital ” of over
<£2,000,000 is represented by landed property;
one-third by Government securities; leaving only
five-twelfths, or over .£833,333 odd, in absolutely
liquid form. Suppose a run occurred on that
bank, would it not direly need the combined
assistance of all the other Associated Banks, as
lately agreed upon ?

A London paper has a story about a well-
known publisher coming out of his mansion to
enter his carriage, and recognising one of his
authors shovelling up the snow from the pave-
ment. “Good gracious, M !” exclaimed the
publisher, “you don’t mean to say you are doing
this sort of thing for a living ?” “ And why not ?”
replied the author. “ If you reckon up what you
gave me for my last novel, you’ll see that 5d. an
hour for street-scavenging means a considerable
advance in the price I get for my work.”
THEY SANG ON THE SHIP’S DECK.
“ Home, home, sweet, sweet home ;
Bo it ever so humble there’s no place like home.”
Blessed be the memory of John Howard Payne
for writing this song. Who doesn’t know it?
Who hasn’t sung it ? Fall of hope, full of heart-
break, its melody has girdled the globe. In July,
1891, a large party of Cornish people sat on the
deck of the good ship City of Paris, bound from
New York to Liverpool. They were coming on a
visit home after long absence. And as the ship
ploughed the calm waters, they sang “Home,
Sweet Home.” But a gale came on, and for two
days most of them had other matters to occupy
their attention; they were too seasick to sing.
Then the Welsh coast was in sight, and they sang
once more, louder than ever. And so it goes on
everywhere—illness and trouble spoil the music.
“My wife never knew ivhat it was to be icell.”
Thus writes a husband, and here’s the rest of his
letter. “She always felt tired and languid,” he
says, “and never got enough rest. She had a
dreadful bad taste in the mouth, with a sinking,
gnawing feeling at her chest, and her food seemed
to do her no good. She had also a dry hacking
cough which people said was a “ Churchyard
Gough. ” She went as thin as a shadow and had
to alter her clothes and make them smaller. Now
better and now worse she went on suffering
like this for ten years, and took all kinds of
medicines, cod liver oil among the rest. She saw
several doctors who sounded her chest and asked
if any of her relatives had died of consumption,
leaving the impression on her mind that she was
consumptive herself. Now as her mother had
died of consumption she felt sure she was going
into a decline. Three years ago I was much con-
cerned about her. She had sick headache all the
time, pain in the sides and legs, and used often to
faint, and got so weak I had to take her in my
arms and lift her in and out of bed. People asked
what was the matter with her, but she could
scarcely describe her condition, for it was an all-
gone feeling, making her fit to drop down. Even
going upstairs would exhaust her and make her
feel faint.
In November, 1883, a book was left at the
house, in which my wife read of a case exactly like
hers, and she exclaimed : “That is just my com-
plaint, and as it has been cured by Mother Seigel’s
Syrup perhaps my ailment is not consumption
after all.”
“I got the Syrup and she found relief from the
first bottle, and soon grew quite strong. The
hacking cough left her and she now enjoys her
food. I consider that a bottle of Seigel’s Syrup is
worth more than a week at the seaside. Before
taking this medicine my wife’s life was a misery
to her. You are at liberty to publish this state-
ment if you desire to do so.”
(Signed) J. Jay Smith, Compositor, 25, Sudely
Street, Islington, London.
This is another instance of indigestion and
dyspepsia being mistaken for consumption. Fortu-
nately the little book referred to, revealed the
error in time to enable Mrs. Smith to treat her-
self for her real disease and bring about a quick
recovery. But cases with a less happy ending
constantly occur. We would again impress the
fact upon the public that consumption, rheu-
matism, gout, bronchitis, kidney complaint, and
nearly all affections of the throat, lungs, and
nerves are virtually symptoms of indigestion and
dyspepsia, for the reason that they are caused by
a poison in the blood directly resulting from a
torpid state of the stomach, liver, and bowels.
Therefore, however strongly the indications seem
to point towards one of those so-called diseases,
your true course is to take Seigel’ Syrup, which
removes the radical difficulty which lies at the
bottom of nearly all physical suffering, viz.
indigestion and dyspepsia.
Who has not sometimes said, “ Oh, I have such
a headache—I can scarcely see ?” What makes
the head ache ? Well, the disordered nerves, of
course. And what throws the nerves out of
order ? Let us see.
“For fifteen years,” says Mr. W. D. Evans, of
Brynawel, Mount Pleasant, Swansea, “I had
been a great sufferer from indigestion and
flatulency. I had a bad taste in the mouth, with
pain and distress after eating, and my bowels
became much swollen. I felt languid and out of
spirits, and had great pain across the temples,
which at times was so severe I could scarcely see.
I saw several doctors who were unable to give me
more than temporary relief. In December, 1890,
I told a friend of mine, Mr. W. Lloyd. Rising Sun
Hotel, Morriston, how bad I was, and he advised
me to try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. After
taking two bottles all the pain and oppression left
me, and I never felt so well in my life as I have
done since. I eat my meals with relish, and feel
light and comfortable afterwards. This remedy
has worked a miracle on me. It is a blessing to
mankind.”
Headaches proceed from indigestion and dys-
pepsia. Use Seigel’s Syrup and your head will
it on your shoulders like a flower on its stalk.
